reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Model that performs:
    1. Conv3D
    2. HardSwish activation
    3. GroupNorm  
    4. Mean pooling across spatial dimensions
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, num_groups=4, bias=True):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.conv = nn.Conv3d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, bias=bias)
        self.group_norm = nn.GroupNorm(num_groups, out_channels)

    def forward(self, x):
        x = self.conv(x)                             # (B, C, D, H, W)
        x = F.hardswish(x)                           # Nonlinear activation
        x = self.group_norm(x)                       # Normalization over channels
        x = torch.mean(x, dim=[2, 3, 4])             # Mean over spatial dims → (B, C)
        return x

# === Test config ===
batch_size = 1024
in_channels = 3
out_channels = 16
depth, height, width = 16, 32, 32
kernel_size = 4

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, depth, height, width)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size]

Each thread processes ~10933/256 ≈ 42 elements. That's a good workload. We need to loop over spatial dims. Spatial indexing: d in [0,13), h in [0,29), w in [0,29). Flatten index tid = d*29*29 + h*29 + w. Kernel pseudo-code: __global__ void fused_reduction(const float* conv_out, float* sum, float* sumsq, int B, int C, int D, int H, int W) { int b = blockIdx.x / C; int c = blockIdx.x % C; int tid = threadIdx.x; int N = D * H * W; float s = 0.0f; float s2 = 0.0f; float* base = conv_out + ((b * C + c) * D * H * W); for (int i = tid; i < N; i += blockDim.x) { float x = base[i]; // hardswish x = x * fminf(fmaxf(x + 3.0f, 0.0f), 6.0f) / 6.0f; s += x; s2 += x * x; } // reduce within block __shared__ float shared_sum[256]; __shared__ float shared_sumsq[256]; shared_sum[tid] = s; shared_sumsq[tid] = s2; __syncthreads(); // reduction... for (int stride = blockDim.x/2; stride > 0; stride /= 2) { if (tid < stride) { shared_sum[tid] += shared_sum[tid + stride]; shared_sumsq[tid] += shared_sumsq[tid + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } if (tid == 0) { sum[blockIdx.x] = shared_sum[0]; sumsq[blockIdx.x] = shared_sumsq[0]; } } Then a second kernel or PyTorch computation to produce final output. Let's check if this is correct numerically. HardSwish: PyTorch's F.hardswish(x) is defined as: x * torch.clamp(x + 3, 0, 6) / 6 In float: `x * min(max(x + 3.0f, 0.0f), 6.0f) / 6.0f`. That's what we wrote. But there might be precision differences if we use `fminf`, `fmaxf` vs `min`, `max` in CUDA. We'll use `fminf` and `fmaxf`. Or we can use `x > 0 ? (x < 3 ? x*(x+3)/6 : x) : 0`? Actually the piecewise definition: hardswish(x) = 0 for x <= -3 = x for x >= 3 = x * (x + 3) / 6 for -3 < x < 3 Using `fminf(fmaxf(x + 3.0f, 0.0f), 6.0f) * x / 6.0f` is continuous and differentiable, same as PyTorch. PyTorch uses `x * hardtanh(x+3, 0, 6) / 6`. hardtanh is `min(max(x, min_val), max_val)`. Should be fine. Let's verify PyTorch's hswish: torch.nn.functional.hardswish(x) is equivalent to x * torch.clamp(x + 3, 0, 6) / 6. In CUDA, we need to match exactly. PyTorch's implementation for `clamp` might have slightly different behavior at boundaries? For fp32, `clamp(x+3, 0, 6)` means `min(max(x+3, 0), 6)`. Using `fminf(fmaxf(x + 3.0f, 0.0f), 6.0f)` should match. Now, groupnorm: In PyTorch, GroupNorm does: mean = x.view(N, num_groups, -1).mean(-1, keepdim=True) var = x.view(N, num_groups, -1).var(-1, unbiased=False, keepdim=True) x = (x - mean) / sqrt(var + eps) x = x * weight + bias Where weight is of shape (C_out,) and bias is of shape (C_out,). Our derived formula: spatial_mean_c = sum_c / N_spatial. Wait, groupnorm computes mean over both channels in group and spatial dims. That's mu_g. But we need spatial_mean_c = mean over spatial dims of x_c. Then we can compute mu_g = mean over c in group of spatial_mean_c. Let's re-derive final output. y_c = gamma_c * (x_c - mu_g) * inv_std_g + beta_c. Mean over spatial dims of y_c = gamma_c * inv_std_g * (mean_spatial(x_c) - mu_g) + beta_c. This is correct because mu_g and inv_std_g are constant w.r.t spatial dims. So we need spatial mean of x_c: m_c = sum_c / N_spatial. And group mean: mu_g = mean_{c in group} m_c = (sum_{c in group} sum_c) / (N_spatial * channels_per_group). Let M = N_spatial * channels_per_group. Then mu_g = (sum_{c in group} sum_c) / M. For variance: var_g = mean_{c in group, spatial} (x_c - mu_g)^2. We can compute var_g = (sum_{c in group} sumsq_c) / M - mu_g^2. Proof: mean_{c in group, spatial} x_c^2 = (sum_{c in group} sumsq_c) / M. And var = E[x^2] - (E[x])^2. So yes, var_g = sumsq_group / M - mu_g^2. So we need sum_c and sumsq_c per channel per batch. Then final: mu_g = sum_group_sum / M mean_sq = sum_group_sumsq / M var_g = mean_sq - mu_g * mu_g inv_std = rsqrt(var_g + eps) out_c = gamma_c * inv_std * (sum_c / N_spatial - mu_g) + beta_c Numerical precision: dividing by M and N_spatial. PyTorch's groupnorm computes mean by summing and dividing by M. So using sum_c and sumsq_c is fine as long as we divide similarly. Note: We must be careful about numeric stability. var_g computed as E[x^2] - (E[x])^2 can be slightly negative due to rounding. We should clamp to >=0 before rsqrt. PyTorch likely handles this in its kernel. We can do `var_g = fmaxf(var_g, 0.0f)`. Also, if we compute sumsq in the first kernel, we need to apply hardswish before squaring. That's correct. Let's sketch the code: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cmath> __global__ void reduce_hardswish_sum_sumsq( const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ out_sum, float* __restrict__ out_sumsq, int B, int C, int D, int H, int W) { int total_channels = B * C; int ch = blockIdx.x; // 0 .. B*C - 1 if (ch >= total_channels) return; int b = ch / C; int c = ch % C; int tid = threadIdx.x; int block_size = blockDim.x; int spatial = D * H * W; const float* ptr = input + ch * spatial; float local_sum = 0.0f; float local_sumsq = 0.0f; for (int i = tid; i < spatial; i += block_size) { float x = ptr[i]; float h = x * fminf(fmaxf(x + 3.0f, 0.0f), 6.0f) * (1.0f / 6.0f); local_sum += h; local_sumsq += h * h; } // block reduce extern __shared__ float sdata[]; float s = 0.0f; float sq = 0.0f; // We need to compute group sum and group sumsq from per-channel sums. // Since each thread only handles one channel, to get group sum we need to either // precompute group aggregated sums in shared memory or in a buffer. // Since cpg is small (e.g., 4), each thread can read the other channels in its group. for (int i = 0; i < cpg; ++i) { int ch = b * C + group_start + i; s += sum[ch]; sq += sumsq[ch]; } float mu_g = s / N; float mean_sq = sq / N; float var_g = mean_sq - mu_g * mu_g; if (var_g < 0.0f) var_g = 0.0f; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var_g + eps); torch::Tensor sumsq = torch::empty({B, C}, options); int total_channels = B * C; int threads = 256; int shared_mem = threads * 2 * sizeof(float); reduce_hardswish_sum_sumsq<<<total_channels, threads, shared_mem>>>( conv_out.data_ptr<float>(), sum.data_ptr<float>(), sumsq.data_ptr<float>(), B, C, D, H, W); torch::Tensor out = torch::empty({B, C}, options); int final_threads = 256; int final_blocks = (total_channels + final_threads - 1) / final_threads; compute_final<<<final_blocks, final_threads>>>( sum.data_ptr<float>(), sumsq.data_ptr<float>(), gamma.data_ptr<float>(), beta.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), B, C, num_groups, spatial, eps); return out; } ''' cpp_source = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> torch::Tensor fused_forward(torch::Tensor conv_out, torch::Tensor gamma, torch::Tensor beta, int num_groups, float eps); ''' # Wait, load_inline expects cpp_sources and cuda_sources. But we need to be careful with `load_inline` syntax. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> template <typename T> __device__ __forceinline__ T hardswish(T x) { return x * fminf(fmaxf(x + (T)3.0, (T)0.0), (T)6.0) * (T)(1.0/6.0); } __global__ void reduce_hardswish_sum_sumsq_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ out_sum, float* __restrict__ out_sumsq, int B, int C, int D, int H, int W) { int total_channels = B * C; int ch = blockIdx.x; if (ch >= total_channels) return; int spatial = D * H * W; const float* ptr = input + ch * spatial; int tid = threadIdx.x; int block_size = blockDim.x; float local_sum = 0.0f; float local_sumsq = 0.0f; // unroll by 4 maybe? int i = tid; for (; i + 3 < spatial; i += block_size * 4) { float x0 = ptr[i]; float x1 = ptr[i + block_size]; float x2 = ptr[i + 2 * block_size]; float x3 = ptr[i + 3 * block_size]; float h0 = hardswish(x0); float h1 = hardswish(x1); float h2 = hardswish(x2); float h3 = hardswish(x3); local_sum += h0 + h1 + h2 + h3; local_sumsq += h0 * h0 + h1 * h1 + h2 * h2 + h3 * h3; } for (; i < spatial; i += block_size) { float x = ptr[i]; float h = hardswish(x); local_sum += h; local_sumsq += h * h; } // Use shared memory reduction extern __shared__ float sdata[]; float* s_sum = sdata; float* s_sumsq = sdata + block_size; s_sum[tid] = local_sum; s_sumsq[tid] = local_sumsq; __syncthreads(); for (int s = block_size / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (tid < s) { s_sum[tid] += s_sum[tid + s]; s_sumsq[tid] += s_sumsq[tid + s]; } __syncthreads(); } if (tid == 0) { out_sum[ch] = s_sum[0]; out_sumsq[ch] = s_sumsq[0]; } } __global__ void compute_groupnorm_mean_kernel( const float* __restrict__ sum, const float* __restrict__ sumsq, const float* __restrict__ gamma, const float* __restrict__ beta, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int C, int num_groups, int spatial, float eps) { int total = B * C; int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= total) return; int b = idx / C; int c = idx % C; int cpg = C / num_groups; int g = c / cpg; int group_start = g * cpg; float N = (float)(spatial * cpg); float group_sum = 0.0f; float group_sumsq = 0.0f; int base = b * C; for (int i = 0; i < cpg; ++i) { int ch = base + group_start + i; group_sum += sum[ch]; group_sumsq += sumsq[ch]; } float mu_g = group_sum / N; float mean_sq = group_sumsq / N; float var_g = mean_sq - mu_g * mu_g; if (var_g < 0.0f) var_g = 0.0f; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var_g + eps); float mean_c = sum[idx] / (float)spatial; float out_val = gamma[c] * inv_std * (mean_c - mu_g) + beta[c]; out[idx] = out_val; } torch::Tensor fused_forward_cuda( torch::Tensor conv_out, torch::Tensor gamma, torch::Tensor beta, int num_groups, float eps) { int B = conv_out.size(0); int C = conv_out.size(1); int D = conv_out.size(2); int H = conv_out.size(3); int W = conv_out.size(4); int spatial = D * H * W; auto options = conv_out.options(); torch::Tensor sum = torch::empty({B, C}, options); torch::Tensor sumsq = torch::empty({B, C}, options); int total_channels = B * C; int threads = 256; int shared = threads * 2 * sizeof(float); reduce_hardswish_sum_sumsq_kernel<<<total_channels, threads, shared>>>( conv_out.data_ptr<float>(), sum.data_ptr<float>(), sumsq.data_ptr<float>(), B, C, D, H, W); torch::Tensor out = torch::empty({B, C}, options); int final_threads = 256; int final_blocks = (total_channels + final_threads - 1) / final_threads; compute_groupnorm_mean_kernel<<<final_blocks, final_threads>>>( sum.data_ptr<float>(), sumsq.data_ptr<float>(), gamma.data_ptr<float>(), beta.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), B, C, num_groups, spatial, eps); return out; } ''' cpp_source = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> torch::Tensor fused_forward_cuda(torch::Tensor conv_out, torch::Tensor gamma, torch::Tensor beta, int num_groups, float eps); ''' fused_extension = load_inline( name='fused_hardswish_gn_mean', cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=['fused_forward_cuda'], extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3'], verbose=True, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, num_groups=4, bias=True): super(ModelNew, self).__init__() self.conv = nn.Conv3d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, bias=bias) self.group_norm = nn.GroupNorm(num_groups, out_channels) self.num_groups = num_groups def forward(self, x): x = self.conv(x) # (B, C, D, H, W) # fused: hardswish + group_norm + mean gamma = self.group_norm.weight beta = self.group_norm.bias eps = self.group_norm.eps return fused_extension.fused_forward_cuda(x, gamma, beta, self.num_groups, eps) ``` Let's test compile and correctness. So the full formula is: z[b, c, i, j, k] = ((h[b, c, i, j, k] - mean_g[b, g]) / sqrt(var_g[b, g] + eps)) * gamma[c] + beta[c] final[b, c] = mean over spatial of z[b, c, :, :, :] = ((mean_c[b, c] - mean_g[b, g]) / sqrt(var_g[b, g] + eps)) * gamma[c] + beta[c] So we need gamma and bias parameters too. To implement this: 1. Load conv weight and bias 2. For each spatial position, compute conv output 3. Apply hardswish 4. Accumulate sum and sum of squares 5. Given all this analysis, I think Approach 1 is the safest: use PyTorch's conv3d (cuDNN optimized) and write a custom kernel to fuse hardswish + group_norm + mean_pooling. For the fused kernel: Input: (B, C, D, H, W) = (1024, 16, 13, 29, 29) Output: (B, C) = (1024, 16) gamma, beta: (C,) Kernel design for fused hardswish+GN+mean: - We'll compute per-channel spatial means and per-group spatial means and sum of squares. - One thread block per (B, group) or per (B, block_of_channels) Actually, for GN+mean, we can process as follows: For each batch element b and group g: - We need to compute: - sum_h[b, c] for each c in group g, over spatial dims - sum_h2[b, g] over all c in group g and spatial dims - Then mean_c[b, c] = sum_h[b, c] / spatial_size - mean_g[b, g] = sum_{c in g} sum_h[b, c] / (channels_per_group * spatial_size) - E_h2_g[b, g] = sum_h2[b, g] / (channels_per_group * spatial_size) - var_g = E_h2_g - mean_g^2 - final[b, c] = ((mean_c - mean_g) / sqrt(var_g + eps)) * gamma[c] + beta[c] To compute this efficiently: Option A: One warp/block per (b, c), reduction over spatial. - Blocks: 1024 * 16 = 16384 blocks. - Each block reduces over 10933 elements. - With 128 threads/block, each thread handles 85 elements. - Total threads: 2,097,152. Good. - Grid stride: load input element, apply hardswish, accumulate sum and sum_sq. - Use warp shuffle or shared memory to reduce. - At the end, we have sum_h and sum_h2 for this (b, c). - But then we need to reduce sum_h across channels in a group to get group mean. We could atomically add to global group_sum array, then a second kernel compute final output. Two kernels: 1. `fused_hardswish_reduce_spatial`: for each (b, c), compute hardswish and spatial sum and sum of squares. Write sum_h[b, c] and sum_h2[b, c] to global memory. 2. `compute_final_output`: read per-channel sums and per-channel sum_sq, compute group stats, output final (b, c). But writing/reading sum_h (1024*16) = 64KB and sum_h2 (1024*16*16 channels for group reduction... wait no, we only need sum_h2 per channel, or per group). Actually we need sum_h per channel (1024*16*4B = 64KB) and sum_h2 per group (1024*4*4B = 16KB). Total intermediate: 80KB. That's tiny! The overhead of the second kernel is probably negligible. Alternatively, we could have each block compute sum_h for all channels in its group and sum_h2 for the group, by having the block process all channels in the group. This avoids a second kernel. Let's do one block per (b, g): - block handles group g of batch b - There are 1024 * 4 = 4096 blocks. - Block size: e.g. 128 threads. - Distribute spatial points (10933) among threads, but each thread loops over all channels in the group to compute per-channel sums and group sum of squares. Wait, that means each thread reads the same spatial point for 4 different channels. Memory access pattern: if we read x[b, c, d, h, w] for c=0,1,2,3, the memory is contiguous in channel dimension (for CHW layout... but PyTorch is NCHW). So x[b, c_start + c, d, h, w] are... in NCHW, channels are not contiguous for a fixed spatial position unless we consider that channels are the second dimension. The stride from one channel to next for same spatial is C_stride, which for contiguous tensor is D*H*W = 10933. So channel access is strided by 10933 elements = not contiguous. That's bad. In NCHW, contiguous access is along W, then H, then D, then C. So for a fixed (b, c), reading along W is contiguous. So for a thread processing a spatial line along W for channel c, that's coalesced. But if it needs to read the same spatial position for multiple channels, those are far apart in memory. Processing strategy: - For (b, g), assign threads to different spatial sub-volumes. - Each thread iterates over its assigned spatial positions. - For each spatial position, read values for all channels in the group (not great for coalescing). - Or: each thread processes one channel for all its spatial positions. Then later reduce channel sums within the block. Better: - Each block processes (b, g). - Divide channels in group among warps: with G=4 channels per group, give 1 channel to each of 4 warps. - Each warp processes all 10933 spatial positions for its assigned channel. - Warp does spatial reduction to get sum per channel. - Warp also accumulates sum of squared values (for variance) into a shared memory accumulator that warps atomically add to. - At the end, all warps synchronize, compute group mean and var from shared memory, then write final output for their channel. Block size = 128 (4 warps of 32). - Warp 0: channel c0, processes 10933 spatial positions with 32 threads = 342 per thread. - All 32 threads in warp read contiguous elements along spatial dimension (d, h, w). Actually 10933 is not a multiple of 32, but we can pad or handle remainder. Wait, 10933 = 13 * 29 * 29. W dimension of 29 is not a multiple of 32 either. For coalescing, if we have warp of 32 threads with thread i reading some element, the elements should be contiguous. If thread layout assigns threadIdx.x to the W dimension, but W=29, then only 29 threads in the warp are active for a given (d, h), and 3 are idle. Not ideal but okay. Actually, we can assign each thread to process a subset of the flattened spatial index. Spatial_size = 10933. Each thread in warp processes roughly 10933/32 elements. Memory access pattern: thread i accesses spatial indices i, i+32, i+64, ... In NCHW layout, spatial index `s` maps to (d, h, w) where s = ((d * H) + h) * W + w. Since W=29, consecutive `s` are along W (contiguous). So accessing s, s+32, s+64 etc. means a stride of 32. With 32 threads reading s, s+1, s+2, ... s+31 simultaneously, that IS coalesced. But if each thread reads s, s+32, s+64... with thread i reading i, i+32, i+64... Then at step 0: thread 0 reads 0, thread 1 reads 1, ... thread 31 reads 31. This is a coalesced 32-word read from address &x[0]. Step 1: thread 0 reads 32, thread 1 reads 33, ... coalesced. Yes! This works perfectly. So each warp of 32 threads can read 32 contiguous floats at a time, then stride by 32, iterating over all 10933 elements. Each thread: 1. For its channel c in group g, accumulate sum = Σ hardswish(conv_out[b, c, s]) and sum_sq = Σ hardswish(conv_out[b, c, s])^2. 2. Warp-reduce sum and sum_sq to get per-channel totals. 3. Thread 0 writes per-channel sum to shared memory. 4. Thread 0 atomically adds sum_sq to group sum_sq in shared memory. After all 4 channels done: 5. Compute group mean = group_sum / (4 * 10933) 6. Compute group E[h^2] = group_sum_sq / (4 * 10933) 7. Compute group var = E[h^2] - mean^2 8. For each channel c: output = ((sum_c / 10933 - group_mean) / sqrt(var + eps)) * gamma[c] + beta[c] But wait, we need the conv output! The conv output is in global memory as the result of F.conv3d. This means in Approach 1, we do: x = F.conv3d(input, weight, bias) x = fused_kernel(x, gamma, beta, num_groups) The fused kernel reads x and writes 1024*16 floats. It processes 1024*16*10933 = 174.8M reads. At 4 bytes per float, that's ~700 MB of reads. Writing 64 KB. At 3.35 TB/s, 700MB should take about 0.2ms. That's very fast! The compute in the kernel is minimal (hardswish, reductions, simple arithmetic). So the potential savings from fusing are: - Avoid writing conv output in one kernel and reading in another - If PyTorch's conv writes output and then hardswish reads it, that's 700MB each way - Our approach reads it once in the fused kernel But we're calling conv3d which will write its output. So we save the reads for hardswish, group norm, and mean. In the reference: 1. conv3d: reads input, writes output x1 (700MB write) 2. hardswish: reads x1, writes x2 (700MB read + 700MB write) 3. group_norm: reads x2, computes reduction, writes x3 (700MB read + maybe some temp + 700MB write) 4. mean: reads x3, writes output (700MB read + 64KB write) Total intermediate memory: ~700MB*6 + 64KB = 4.2GB of intermediate memory traffic. Plus conv input reads and weight reads. Our approach: 1. conv3d: reads input, writes x1 (201MB read + 700MB write) 2. fused_kernel: reads x1, writes output (700MB read + 64KB write) Total intermediate: 700MB*2 + 64KB + 201MB read = ~1.6GB intermediate Actually wait, conv also reads weights. But in both cases. So our approach saves approximately 4.2GB - 1.6GB = 2.6GB of memory bandwidth. At 3.35 TB/s that's ~0.78ms saved. Plus kernel launch overhead saved. If the reference takes ~2-3ms, we could save ~30-50% of the time. That's significant! Now, can we do better by fusing conv into the kernel? The conv itself writes 700MB that we then read. If we fuse conv with our reductions, we eliminate both the 700MB write and 700MB read, saving another 1.4GB. Total potential save: 4GB of memory traffic. This could be worth 1.2ms. Plus potentially better computational efficiency. But implementing an efficient conv3d in CUDA is non-trivial. Let me think if it's manageable for this specific case. Actually, let me consider a hybrid approach. What if we compute conv on-the-fly but in a simple way that still benefits from some caching? Conv3d for one output channel c and batch b: For each spatial output position (d, h, w): out = bias[c] for in_c in 0..2: for kd in 0..3: for kh in 0..3: for kw in 0..3: out += input[b, in_c, d+kd, h+kh, w+kw] * weight[c, in_c, kd, kh, kw] If we process all 16 channels for the same output position, we can load the input patch once and reuse it for all 16 channels. But in a warp, if 32 threads are processing 32 different output positions, they each load different input patches. That's 32 different 3×4×4×4 patches = 6144 floats per step, which doesn't fit well in L1 cache and would have huge bandwidth. But if we process output positions close together, they share input values. E.g., positions (d, h, w) and (d, h, w+1) share most of the 4×4×4 patch - only one "column" differs. So processing spatially close positions is key. With a thread block handling a tile of spatial positions, we can load the input tile into shared memory. This is the standard approach. Let me design a fused conv kernel more concretely: Block configuration: - Process a tile of output spatial positions, say Td×Th×Tw = 2×4×4 = 32. - Input footprint: 5×7×7×3 = 735 floats = 2.9KB. - All 16 output channels computed from this tile. - Total output elements per tile: 32 * 16 = 512. - With block size = 128 threads, each thread handles 4 output elements. - Number of tiles: ceil(13/2)*ceil(29/4)*ceil(29/4) = 7*8*8 = 448 - Total blocks: 1024 * 448 = 458,752. That's a lot! At 128 threads each, that's 58M threads. Way too many for context switch overhead. We'd want maybe 256 or 512 threads per block to increase work per block. Let me think differently. What if we process all tiles for a batch element in one block, but loop through tiles? One block per (batch, channel_group): - Block handles all spatial positions for 4 channels. - For spatial positions, process in tiles that fit in shared memory. - Loop over all tiles, loading input for current tile, computing outputs, accumulating stats. With batch=1024, groups=4, total blocks = 4096. That's reasonable. Block size = 128 or 256 threads. Each block processes 10933 spatial positions × 4 channels = 43732 outputs. Each output: 192 FMAs = 8.4M FMAs. At 67 TFLOPS theoretical, ~0.125ms per block if compute-bound. With 4096 blocks and 132 SMs, we'd need ~4 waves if each SM can run multiple blocks simultaneously. If each block takes 0.5ms (including memory), and we have 132 SMs each processing ~31 blocks, total time = ~31 * 0.5ms / overlappability. That's too much. Actually, blocks on different SMs run in parallel. 4096 blocks / 132 SMs = ~31 blocks per SM. If each takes 0.5ms and each SM can run 2 blocks concurrently (due to resource limits), that's ~16 serial chunks = 8ms. That's worse than just using cuDNN! So the direct conv approach with resource-heavy blocks is risky. cuDNN has very clever algorithms and uses tensor cores where possible. Beating it with a simple custom kernel is hard. Let me reconsider and go with the pragmatic approach: use cuDNN for conv3d, write a fused kernel for hardswish + GN + mean. For the fused kernel, we need to handle 174.8M elements and reduce them. Wait, is there an even simpler approach? Torch provides `torch.nn.functional.group_norm`. Could we use that but fuse mean into it? Or even just use `torch.compile`? No, we need a custom CUDA kernel. Let me write the fused reduction kernel: ```python torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline( cuda_sources=''' __global__ void fused_hardswish_gn_mean_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ gamma, const float* __restrict__ beta, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C, int D, int H, int W, int num_groups, float eps) { int b = blockIdx.x; int c = blockIdx.y; int g = c / (C / num_groups); int spatial_size = D * H * W; int idx = (b * C + c) * spatial_size; float sum = 0.0f; float sum_sq = 0.0f; // Grid-stride loop over spatial dimensions for (int s = threadIdx.x; s < spatial_size; s += blockDim.x) { float x = input[idx + s]; // hardswish x = x * fminf(fmaxf(x + 3.0f, 0.0f), 6.0f) / 6.0f; sum += x; sum_sq += x * x; } __shared__ float shared_sum[32]; __shared__ float shared_sum_sq[32]; __shared__ float group_sum; __shared__ float group_sum_sq; // Warp reduction for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); sum_sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum_sq, offset); } if (threadIdx.x % 32 == 0) { shared_sum[threadIdx.x / 32] = sum; shared_sum_sq[threadIdx.x / 32] = sum_sq; } __syncthreads(); if (threadIdx.x < 32) { float lane_sum = (threadIdx.x < (blockDim.x + 31) / 32) ? shared_sum[threadIdx.x] : 0.0f; float lane_sq = (threadIdx.x < (blockDim.x + 31) / 32) ? shared_sum_sq[threadIdx.x] : 0.0f; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { lane_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, lane_sum, offset); lane_sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, lane_sq, offset); } if (threadIdx.x == 0) { shared_sum[0] = lane_sum; shared_sum_sq[0] = lane_sq; } } __syncthreads(); float chan_sum = shared_sum[0]; float chan_sq_sum = shared_sum_sq[0]; // Now we need group-level sum across channels. // AtomicAdd to global memory for group accumulation. // But atomic adds from different blocks for same (b, g) race. extern __shared__ float s[]; float* group_sums = s; float* group_sq_sums = s + num_groups; if (threadIdx.x == 0) { group_sums[g] = chan_sum; group_sq_sums[g] = chan_sq_sum; } __syncthreads(); // Wait, this is per-block shared memory so doesn't help across blocks. // Each (b, c) is a separate block. We need global atomics or separate kernel. } ''' ) ``` Hmm, with one block per (b, c), each (b, g) has 4 blocks. They would need to coordinate to compute group statistics. This requires either: 1. A second kernel that reads per-channel sums and computes final output 2. Atomic operations on global memory 3. Cooperative groups or grid synchronization (complex) Second kernel is cleanest. Two small kernels: Kernel 1: For each (b, c), compute channel sum and sum_sq after hardswish. Write to global arrays of size (B, C). Kernel 2: For each (b, c), read channel sums for its group, compute group stats, write final output. Memory for intermediates: 2 * B * C floats = 2 * 1024 * 16 * 4 = 131 KB. Negligible. But wait, we can do better with one block per (b, g) where the block reads all channels in the group and computes everything in one go. Grid: (B=1024, G=4) = 4096 blocks Block: 128 or 256 threads. Each thread loops over spatial positions for... all channels in group, or just one channel? If each thread handles all channels at each spatial position: - Each thread reads spatial point s and then values at channels c0, c1, c2, c3 for that s. - In NCHW, these are strided by spatial_size = 10933. Bad memory coalescing. If each thread handles one channel: - With 128 threads and 4 channels, we could assign 32 threads per channel. - Each of the 32 threads reads contiguous segments of spatial data for its channel. - Coalescing is good within each channel. Approach: - Block for (b, g). - 4 warps: warp 0 for channel c0, warp 1 for c1, etc. - Each warp (32 threads) processes all 10933 spatial positions for its channel. - Thread i in warp processes positions i, i+32, i+64, ... - Warp reduces to get per-channel sum and sum_sq. After all warps have their channel sums and sum_sq: - Communicate via shared memory. - Thread 0 computes group_sum = sum of 4 channel sums, group_sum_sq = sum of 4 channel sum_sq. - Broadcast group stats. - Each warp computes final output for its channel. This is elegant! Block size: 4 warps = 128 threads. But wait, can we use more threads? We have 10933 positions per channel, and 32 threads per channel gives 342 iterations per thread. That's okay but not huge. With 128 threads per channel, each thread does only 85 iterations. Better! But then with 4 channels, we need 512 threads per block. 512 threads per block is fine. 4096 blocks * 512 threads = 2,097,152 threads. Still plenty. With 128 threads per channel: - 4 channels * 128 threads = 512 threads total. - In practice, this is 16 warps. But warps naturally work on the same channel. But how to organize? We could have the block dimension be 128, and process channels sequentially. Or we could have logical grouping by channel. Actually, since NCHW has stride spatial_size between channels, it's more natural to process one channel at a time with many threads. Block size = 256 threads. For channel c: - All 256 threads cooperate to reduce spatial dimension. - Each thread reads spatial positions at stride 256. - Warp shuffle + shared memory reduction. - Then proceed to next channel in the group. - This sequentializes channels but is easy to implement. With 4 channels sequentially and 10933 spatial positions per channel at 256 threads: - Per channel: ceil(10933/256) = 43 loads per thread. - 4 channels * 43 = 172 loads per thread. Fine. In fact, with this approach: - 4096 blocks - Each block does (4 channels * 10933 spatial points * hardswish + reduction + some math) - Total loads per block: 4 * 10933 = 43732 floats = 175 KB - Fits in L1/L2 cache. Actually, 4096 blocks each loading 175 KB from different memory locations. Total working set: 4096 * 175 KB = 716 MB. That's exactly the conv output size. Good, it fits in device memory and caches well. Wait, but 4096 blocks all running concurrently: if each block uses 256 threads, that's 1M threads. On H100 with 132 SMs and max 2048 threads per SM (actually 1536 or 2048), we might fit about 2048*132 = 270K threads concurrently. So 1M threads would take ~4 waves. Fine. Per-block shared memory: just enough for reductions. 256 threads → 8 warps. We need to reduce 8 warp-level sums. Shared memory: maybe 32 floats. Negligible. Kernel memory bandwidth: each conv output element is read once. 700 MB total. At 3.35 TB/s, this kernel itself is ~0.2ms. The overhead is startup and reductions. Now, can we make it even faster? One issue: the conv output was already written and we're reading it. If conv takes ~1ms and our kernel takes 0.2ms, total is ~1.2ms. Reference might be conv (1ms) + hardswish (0.1ms if fused with read) + GN (0.3ms) + mean (0.1ms) = ~1.5ms. We'd get about 20% speedup. If we could also fuse conv, we might save more. But let's start with the simpler approach and see. Actually, looking at the kernel more carefully: PyTorch's group norm is actually quite optimized in recent versions, and hardswish is fused into the elementwise kernel automatically via optimizations. But the mean pool is a separate reduction. The real win might come from avoiding multiple kernel boundaries and the intermediate memory round trips. Let me implement the approach: 1. `conv3d` in PyTorch 2. Custom kernel for `hardswish + group_norm + mean` fused But wait, there's another fusion opportunity! The group norm has weight and bias. In the reference, `self.group_norm` is created with default `affine=True`, so it learns `weight` and `bias`. We need to use these in our kernel. Also, the reference `self.conv` is created with `bias=True` by default. The conv weight and bias are accessible as `self.conv.weight` and `self.conv.bias`. Now, for `ModelNew`, we need to accept the same constructor args and set up the conv in the same way. We can even keep it as a `nn.Conv3d` layer and use it in forward, then call our custom kernel. Let me write the code: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, num_groups=4, bias=True): super().__init__() self.conv = nn.Conv3d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, bias=bias) self.group_norm = nn.GroupNorm(num_groups, out_channels) # Load custom CUDA extension self.fused_kernels = load_kernels() def forward(self, x): x = self.conv(x) # (B, C, D, H, W) # Call fused kernel: hardswish + GN + mean B, C, D, H, W = x.shape num_groups = self.group_norm.num_groups gamma = self.group_norm.weight beta = self.group_norm.bias eps = self.group_norm.eps out = torch.empty(B, C, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype) self.fused_kernels.fused_forward( x, gamma, beta, out, B, C, D, H, W, num_groups, eps ) return out ``` Now let me write the actual CUDA kernel. I'll do a single kernel that fuses everything. Actually, we can write it as a single kernel with grid (B, G) and 128/256 threads. Each block handles one group and computes final outputs for all channels in the group. ```cpp __global__ void fused_hardswish_gn_mean_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ gamma, const float* __restrict__ beta, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C, int D, int H, int W, int num_groups, float eps) { int b = blockIdx.x; int g = blockIdx.y; int channels_per_group = C / num_groups; int c_start = g * channels_per_group; int spatial_size = D * H * W; // For each channel in the group, compute spatial sum and sum of squares float sum[channels_per_group]; // but we can't do VLA in CUDA like this if not known at compile time... ``` channels_per_group = C / num_groups. C=16, num_groups=4, so channels_per_group=4. This is a compile-time constant if we want, but the kernel should be generic. We can just unroll for small values or use a loop. Wait, we can use shared memory for per-channel accumulators. ```cpp extern __shared__ float s[]; float* sh_sum = s; // channels_per_group floats float* sh_sum_sq = s + channels_per_group; // group-level sq sum (1 float) ``` Actually if channels_per_group is small (like 4), we can just use registers. Let me design with a fixed assumption that channels_per_group is small, or make it general: ```cpp float local_sum[4]; // max 4, or we can dynamically index registers via loop ``` But channels_per_group isn't necessarily known at compile time. However, in practical GPU kernels, the compiler usually handles small constant loop unrolling well even if it's technically a runtime value. A cleaner approach: process channels sequentially within the block. ```cpp // Shared memory for inter-warp reduction __shared__ float sh_sum[32]; // enough for 32 warps __shared__ float sh_sq[32]; __shared__ float group_sum_val; __shared__ float group_sum_sq_val; __shared__ float group_mean; __shared__ float group_var; float spatial_sum = 0.0f; float spatial_sq = 0.0f; int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; for (int rel_c = 0; rel_c < channels_per_group; ++rel_c) { int c = c_start + rel_c; int base_idx = (b * C + c) * spatial_size; spatial_sum = 0.0f; spatial_sq = 0.0f; // Grid-stride over spatial dimension for (int s = threadIdx.x; s < spatial_size; s += blockDim.x) { float x = input[base_idx + s]; x = x * fminf(fmaxf(x + 3.0f, 0.0f), 6.0f) / 6.0f; spatial_sum += x; spatial_sq += x * x; } // Block reduction using warp shuffle then shared memory // ... standard reduction ... // After reduction, spatial_sum is the channel sum // We'll write the channel final output later } ``` Wait, but we need group statistics before we can compute final output for each channel. So we need to: 1. For each channel in group, compute spatial_sum (sum over spatial of hardswish(x)) 2. Compute group_sum = sum of all channel spatial_sums 3. Compute group_sum_sq = sum of all channel spatial_sq_sums 4. Compute group_mean and group_var 5. For each channel, final[b, c] = ((spatial_sum/spatial_size - group_mean) / sqrt(var+eps)) * gamma[c] + beta[c] So we need to store spatial_sum per channel during the loop, and accumulate group_sum and group_sum_sq. If channels_per_group = 4, we can store channel sums in shared memory. ```cpp __shared__ float channel_sums[4]; __shared__ float channel_sq_sums[4]; // only need group total though ``` Actually we only need the per-channel sum for the final output formula. We need the group total of sums and the group total of sum_sq. Let me write a cleaner version with a 2-step approach within the kernel: ```cpp __global__ void fused_kernel(...) { int b = blockIdx.x; int g = blockIdx.y; int cpg = C / num_groups; int c0 = g * cpg; int spatial_size = D * H * W; // Phase 1: compute per-channel spatial sums and group sum of squares __shared__ float sh channel_sums[16]; // up to 16 channels per group max __shared__ float sh group_sq_sum; __shared__ float sh group_sum; __shared__ float sh group_mean; __shared__ float sh group_var; float my_sum = 0.0f; float my_sq_sum = 0.0f; for (int rc = 0; rc < cpg; rc++) { int c = c0 + rc; int base = (b * C + c) * spatial_size; float sum = 0.0f; float sq = 0.0f; for (int s = threadIdx.x; s < spatial_size; s += blockDim.x) { float x = input[base + s]; x = x * fminf(fmaxf(x + 3.0f, 0.0f), 6.0f) / 6.0f; sum += x; sq += x * x; } // Reduce within block // After reducing, store channel sum in shared // Atomically add sq to group_sq_sum } } ``` But the block reduction needs to happen for each channel. Let me write a helper function or inline it. ```cpp __device__ inline float block_reduce_sum(float val) { // Warp reduce for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); // Store to shared __shared__ float shared[32]; int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; if (lane == 0) shared[warp_id] = val; __syncthreads(); // Final reduce in warp 0 val = (threadIdx.x < blockDim.x / 32) ? shared[threadIdx.x] : 0.0f; if (warp_id == 0) { for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); } __syncthreads(); return val; } ``` Wait, but after this function, only thread 0 has the correct value? We need all threads to know it, or at least broadcast it. Actually we'd want it broadcast. But for our use case, we just need one thread to write to shared memory. ```cpp __device__ inline float warp_reduce_sum(float val) { for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); return val; } ``` Then in the kernel: ```cpp float sum = ...; float sq = ...; sum = warp_reduce_sum(sum); sq = warp_reduce_sum(sq); __shared__ float warp_sums[32]; __shared__ float warp_sq_sums[32]; __shared__ float final_sum; __shared__ float final_sq; int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; if (lane == 0) { warp_sums[warp_id] = sum; warp_sq_sums[warp_id] = sq; } __syncthreads(); if (threadIdx.x < (blockDim.x + 31) / 32) { sum = warp_sums[threadIdx.x]; sq = warp_sq_sums[threadIdx.x]; sum = warp_reduce_sum(sum); sq = warp_reduce_sum(sq); if (threadIdx.x == 0) { final_sum = sum; final_sq = sq; } } __syncthreads(); float block_sum = final_sum; float block_sq = final_sq; ``` Now, with this, each channel reduction gives `block_sum` and `block_sq` which are available to all threads via shared memory. In our kernel, for each channel: - All threads contribute to the reduction - After sync, all threads see the result - Thread 0 writes channel sum to shared array, adds sq to group_sq accumulator - After all channels, compute group stats - Then one thread per channel writes final output Let me write the full kernel: ```cpp __global__ void fused_hardswish_gn_mean_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ gamma, const float* __restrict__ beta, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C, int D, int H, int W, int num_groups, float eps) { extern __shared__ float sh_mem[]; // Layout: channel_sums[cpg], group_sq_sum, group_sum, group_mean, group_var float* channel_sums = sh_mem; float* group_sq_sum_ptr = sh_mem + 16; // max channels per group float* group_sum_ptr = group_sq_sum_ptr + 1; float* group_mean_ptr = group_sum_ptr + 1; float* group_var_ptr = group_mean_ptr + 1; int b = blockIdx.x; int g = blockIdx.y; int cpg = C / num_groups; int c0 = g * cpg; int spatial_size = D * H * W; if (threadIdx.x == 0) { *group_sq_sum_ptr = 0.0f; *group_sum_ptr = 0.0f; } __syncthreads(); for (int rc = 0; rc < cpg; ++rc) { int c = c0 + rc; int base = (b * C + c) * spatial_size; float sum = 0.0f; float sq = 0.0f; // Grid-stride loop for (int s = threadIdx.x; s < spatial_size; s += blockDim.x) { float x = input[base + s]; x = x * fminf(fmaxf(x + 3.0f, 0.0f), 6.0f) / 6.0f; sum += x; sq += x * x; } // Block-level reduction __shared__ float warp_sums[32]; __shared__ float warp_sq_sums[32]; __shared__ float red_sum; __shared__ float red_sq; // Warp reduce for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sq, offset); } int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; int num_warps = (blockDim.x + 31) / 32; if (lane == 0) { warp_sums[warp_id] = sum; warp_sq_sums[warp_id] = sq; } __syncthreads(); if (threadIdx.x < num_warps) { sum = warp_sums[threadIdx.x]; sq = warp_sq_sums[threadIdx.x]; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sq, offset); } if (threadIdx.x == 0) { red_sum = sum; red_sq = sq; } } __syncthreads(); float chan_sum = red_sum; if (threadIdx.x == 0) { channel_sums[rc] = chan_sum; atomicAdd(group_sq_sum_ptr, red_sq); atomicAdd(group_sum_ptr, chan_sum); } __syncthreads(); } // Compute group stats if (threadIdx.x == 0) { float gs = *group_sum_ptr; float gsq = *group_sq_sum_ptr; float N = float(cpg * spatial_size); float mean = gs / N; float mean_sq = gsq / N; float var = mean_sq - mean * mean; var = fmaxf(var, 0.0f); *group_mean_ptr = mean; *group_var_ptr = var; } __syncthreads(); float mean = *group_mean_ptr; float var = *group_var_ptr; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); float spatial_inv = 1.0f / float(spatial_size); // Write final output for (int rc = 0; rc < cpg; ++rc) { int c = c0 + rc; float chan_spatial_mean = channel_sums[rc] * spatial_inv; float normalized = (chan_spatial_mean - mean) * inv_std; float out_val = normalized * gamma[c] + beta[c]; if (threadIdx.x == 0) { output[b * C + c] = out_val; } } } ``` There are a few issues: 1. The `atomicAdd` for float might be slow, but with only 4 per block and 4096 blocks, it's probably fine. 2. We run the channel loop `cpg` times. If cpg=4, there are 4 iterations. Each iteration does a full block sync reduction. With 4096 blocks, total reductions = 16384. Each reduction is very fast (warp shuffle + one more step). 3. But atomics within shared memory... actually `atomicAdd` on shared memory for float is not available in all compute capabilities. Shared memory atomicAdd for float requires CC 6.0+. H100 is 9.0, so it's fine. Actually, instead of atomics, thread 0 could just accumulate after the reductions: ```cpp if (threadIdx.x == 0) { channel_sums[rc] = red_sum; channel_sq_sums[rc] = red_sq; // need this array } __syncthreads(); ``` After the loop, thread 0 sums up all channel_sq_sums: ```cpp if (threadIdx.x == 0) { float gsq = 0; float gs = 0; for (int rc = 0; rc < cpg; ++rc) { gs += channel_sums[rc]; gsq += channel_sq_sums[rc]; } *group_sum_ptr = gs; *group_sq_sum_ptr = gsq; } ``` This avoids atomics and is cleaner! Let me revise: ```cpp __global__ void fused_hardswish_gn_mean_kernel(...) { extern __shared__ float sh[]; float* channel_sums = sh; float* channel_sq_sums = sh + 16; float* group_mean_ptr = sh + 32; float* group_var_ptr = sh + 33; int b = blockIdx.x; int g = blockIdx.y; int cpg = C / num_groups; int c0 = g * cpg; int spatial_size = D * H * W; for (int rc = 0; rc < cpg; ++rc) { int c = c0 + rc; int base = (b * C + c) * spatial_size; float sum = 0.0f, sq = 0.0f; for (int s = threadIdx.x; s < spatial_size; s += blockDim.x) { float x = input[base + s]; x = x * fminf(fmaxf(x + 3.0f, 0.0f), 6.0f) / 6.0f; sum += x; sq += x * x; } // block reduce __shared__ float warp_s[32]; __shared__ float warp_q[32]; __shared__ float rs; __shared__ float rq; for (int off = 16; off > 0; off >>= 1) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, off); sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sq, off); } int wid = threadIdx.x >> 5; int lane = threadIdx.x & 31; if (lane == 0) { warp_s[wid] = sum; warp_q[wid] = sq; } __syncthreads(); int nw = (blockDim.x + 31) >> 5; if (threadIdx.x < nw) { sum = warp_s[threadIdx.x]; sq = warp_q[threadIdx.x]; for (int off = 16; off > 0; off >>= 1) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, off); sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sq, off); } if (threadIdx.x == 0) { rs = sum; rq = sq; } } __syncthreads(); if (threadIdx.x == 0) { channel_sums[rc] = rs; channel_sq_sums[rc] = rq; } __syncthreads(); } if (threadIdx.x == 0) { float gs = 0.0f, gsq = 0.0f; for (int rc = 0; rc < cpg; ++rc) { gs += channel_sums[rc]; gsq += channel_sq_sums[rc]; } float N = float(cpg * spatial_size); float mean = gs / N; float mean_sq = gsq / N; float var = mean_sq - mean * mean; var = fmaxf(var, 0.0f); *group_mean_ptr = mean; *group_var_ptr = var; } __syncthreads(); float mean = *group_mean_ptr; float var = *group_var_ptr; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); float sp_inv = 1.0f / float(spatial_size); if (threadIdx.x < cpg) { int c = c0 + threadIdx.x; float cmean = channel_sums[threadIdx.x] * sp_inv; float out = ((cmean - mean) * inv_std) * gamma[c] + beta[c]; output[b * C + c] = out; } } ``` Shared memory usage: 16 + 16 + 2 = 34 floats = 136 bytes. Negligible. Block size: 128 or 256 threads. With spatial_size=10933: - Block size 128: each thread does ceil(10933/128) = 86 iterations per channel. 4 channels = 344 loads per thread. Total loads per block = 128 * 344 = 44032, but each is coalesced ( accesses base + threadIdx.x + k*blockDim.x ). Actually 128 threads read 128 contiguous floats at a time. Great coalescing. - Block size 256: 43 loads per channel, 172 total per thread. Also good. I'll use 256 threads to have fewer loads per thread. Wait, there's another issue. The `for (int rc = 0; rc < cpg; ++rc)` loop means all threads in the block process channel 0 together, then channel 1 together, etc. But in NCHW layout, consecutive channels are spaced by `spatial_size` elements. So when we move from channel 0 to channel 1, all threads jump by `spatial_size` floats. This means at each step, threads still read contiguous elements within a channel. That's fine! Coalescing is preserved. What about occupancy? 4096 blocks, 256 threads each. Warp count = 16 warps per block. Shared memory = ~136 bytes. Registers: each thread has maybe ~20-30 floats (lane, warp_id, sum, sq, loop vars, etc.) = 80-120 bytes = 20-30 registers. Total ~5K-8K per block. H100 can have many concurrent blocks. Actually, register usage might be closer to 40-50 due to the compiler. Still well under the 64K limit. This kernel should be very efficient. The main bottleneck is reading the conv output from global memory. Let me think if there's any way to further optimize. We could use `float4` loads to process 4 floats per thread per iteration. But 10933 is not divisible by 4, so we'd need a remainder loop. With float4: ```cpp for (int s = threadIdx.x; s < spatial_size / 4; s += blockDim.x) { float4 x4 = ((const float4*)(input + base))[s]; // process each element } ``` But 10933/4 = 2733.25, not clean. We could pad the conv output to a multiple of 4, or just handle it. Actually, doing float4 requires the base pointer to be aligned, and `spatial_size` to be a multiple of 4. 10933 is not a multiple of 4. We can pad the conv output or just not use float4. What about vectorized loads with float2? 10933 is odd, so still not great. I think standard float loads with good coalescing are fine. The GPU memory controllers will combine contiguous accesses automatically. Let me also consider using `__ldg()` for read-only data: `float x = __ldg(input + base + s);` This is hint to use texture/L1 cache. Since input is read-only in this kernel, this might help. Now for the conv itself. Can we call cuDNN's conv3d and get the output with padding to help coalescing? No, we just use `self.conv(x)`. One more optimization: since we know the exact dimensions (though the kernel should be generic), is there any benefit from compile-time constants? We can pass the runtime dimensions and the compiler will unroll loops and compute constants where possible. Actually, let me check if Torch actually fuses the hardswish into the conv backward or something... no, we only care about forward. Now, what about numerical precision? The formula for variance: var = E[x^2] - (E[x])^2 This can suffer from catastrophic cancellation if the variance is small compared to the mean. PyTorch uses Welford's online algorithm for better numerical stability. Should I use Welford's algorithm instead? With 10933 elements and 4 channels, the total group size is 43732. Welford's algorithm would do: ```cpp float mean = 0.0f; float M2 = 0.0f; int count = 0; for each x: count++; float delta = x - mean; mean += delta / count; float delta2 = x - mean; M2 += delta * delta2; var = M2 / count; ``` Welford requires one pass and avoids the catastrophic cancellation issue. It also requires accumulating `mean` and `M2`, then at the end `var = M2 / count`. But in our case, we need both the per-channel mean (sum) AND the group variance. For group variance using Welford across all channels in the group, we'd need a two-level approach. Actually, Welford is a bit trickier when done in parallel. There are parallel implementations (Chan et al.), but for simplicity and since this is fp32 with moderate-sized data, the two-pass approach might be fine. However, PyTorch's group norm uses Welford for numerical stability. For correctness, we should match PyTorch's numerical results within tolerance (1e-4 for fp32). With inputs that are conv outputs (potentially large values) and 4 channels of 10933 elements, the variance could be small relative to the squared mean. The two-pass `E[x^2] - E[x]^2` could lose precision. Should I use Welford? Let me think about how to parallelize Welford. A parallel Welford reduction works as follows: Each thread computes local mean and M2 for its subset. Then we combine pairs using the formula: Given (n1, mean1, M2_1) and (n2, mean2, M2_2): n = n1 + n2 delta = mean2 - mean1 mean = mean1 + delta * n2 / n M2 = M2_1 + M2_2 + delta * delta * n1 * n2 / n This can be warp-reduced with shuffle operations. For our kernel, for each channel: - Each thread computes local mean and M2 over its subset of spatial positions. - Warp-reduce to get per-warp (n, mean, M2). - Block-reduce to get per-channel (spatial_size, mean_c, M2_c). - Then channel variance var_c = M2_c / spatial_size. For group-level: we need to combine all channels in the group. Since each channel has n = spatial_size, and there are cpg channels: - We can combine (spatial_size, mean_c, M2_c) for all channels in the group. - This gives group (cpg * spatial_size, group_mean, group_M2). - group_var = group_M2 / (cpg * spatial_size). The final output for channel c: out = ((mean_c - group_mean) / sqrt(group_var + eps)) * gamma[c] + beta[c] This is numerically equivalent to what we derived earlier, but computed stably. Let me implement Welford. It's actually quite clean and I think it's better for correctness. ```cpp struct WelfordData { float n; float mean; float m2; }; __device__ inline WelfordData welford_combine(WelfordData a, WelfordData b) { WelfordData res; res.n = a.n + b.n; if (res.n == 0.0f) { res.mean = 0.0f; res.m2 = 0.0f; return res; } float delta = b.mean - a.mean; res.mean = a.mean + delta * b.n / res.n; res.m2 = a.m2 + b.m2 + delta * delta * a.n * b.n / res.n; return res; } __device__ inline WelfordData warp_reduce_welford(WelfordData val) { for (int offset = 1; offset < 32; offset <<= 1) { WelfordData other; other.n = __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val.n, offset); other.mean = __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val.mean, offset); other.m2 = __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val.m2, offset); val = welford_combine(val, other); } return val; } ``` Wait, the standard `__shfl_down_sync` with offset 16, 8, 4, 2, 1 might be more efficient than 1, 2, 4, ... for reduction. But shuffle latency is only a few cycles. Actually it doesn't matter much, but powers of 2 from half-warp down is standard. Actually, for reduction, we want: ```cpp for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { float n = __shfl_down_sync(mask, val.n, offset); // combine } ``` This tree-style reduction has log2(32) = 5 steps. Here's the Welford block reduction: ```cpp __device__ inline void block_reduce_welford(WelfordData val, WelfordData* out) { WelfordData lane_res = warp_reduce_welford(val); __shared__ WelfordData warp_data[32]; int lane = threadIdx.x & 31; int warp_id = threadIdx.x >> 5; if (lane == 0) warp_data[warp_id] = lane_res; __syncthreads(); WelfordData block_res = {0, 0, 0}; int num_warps = (blockDim.x + 31) >> 5; if (threadIdx.x < num_warps) { block_res = warp_data[threadIdx.x]; // warp reduce again among the warp leaders block_res = warp_reduce_welford(block_res); } if (threadIdx.x == 0) *out = block_res; __syncthreads(); } ``` This looks clean. Let me assemble the full kernel. ```cpp __global__ void fused_hardswish_gn_mean_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ gamma, const float* __restrict__ beta, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C, int D, int H, int W, int num_groups, float eps) { struct Welford { float n; float mean; float m2; }; auto welford_combine = [](Welford a, Welford b) -> Welford { Welford r; r.n = a.n + b.n; float delta = b.mean - a.mean; r.mean = a.mean + delta * b.n / r.n; r.m2 = a.m2 + b.m2 + delta * delta * a.n * b.n / r.n; return r; }; auto warp_reduce = [&](Welford val) -> Welford { for (int off = 16; off > 0; off >>= 1) { Welford o; o.n = __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val.n, off); o.mean = __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val.mean, off); o.m2 = __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val.m2, off); val = welford_combine(val, o); } return val; }; extern __shared__ float sh[]; float* chan_means = sh; // We'll store per-channel mean, and use shared memory for warp data and group result int b = blockIdx.x; int g = blockIdx.y; int cpg = C / num_groups; int c0 = g * cpg; int spatial_size = D * H * W; __shared__ Welford group_res; __shared__ float final_mean; __shared__ float final_var; __shared__ Welford warp_buf[32]; Welford group_acc = {0, 0, 0}; for (int rc = 0; rc < cpg; ++rc) { int c = c0 + rc; int base = (b * C + c) * spatial_size; Welford acc = {0, 0, 0}; for (int s = threadIdx.x; s < spatial_size; s += blockDim.x) { float x = __ldg(input + base + s); x = x * fminf(fmaxf(x + 3.0f, 0.0f), 6.0f) / 6.0f; acc.n += 1.0f; float delta = x - acc.mean; acc.mean += delta / acc.n; float delta2 = x - acc.mean; acc.m2 += delta * delta2; } Welford lane_res = warp_reduce(acc); int lane = threadIdx.x & 31; int wid = threadIdx.x >> 5; if (lane == 0) warp_buf[wid] = lane_res; __syncthreads(); Welford block_res = {0, 0, 0}; int nw = (blockDim.x + 31) >> 5; if (threadIdx.x < nw) { block_res = warp_buf[threadIdx.x]; block_res = warp_reduce(block_res); } if (threadIdx.x == 0) { chan_means[rc] = block_res.mean; group_acc = welford_combine(group_acc, block_res); } __syncthreads(); } if (threadIdx.x == 0) { group_res = group_acc; final_mean = group_acc.mean; final_var = (group_acc.n > 0) ? (group_acc.m2 / group_acc.n) : 0.0f; } __syncthreads(); float mean = final_mean; float var = fmaxf(final_var, 0.0f); float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); if (threadIdx.x < cpg) { int c = c0 + threadIdx.x; float cmean = chan_means[threadIdx.x]; float out = ((cmean - mean) * inv_std) * gamma[c] + beta[c]; output[b * C + c] = out; } } ``` Wait, `group_acc` is a local variable to all threads, but only thread 0 writes the group combine result. Other threads have stale `group_acc`. But then we have `__syncthreads()` and read from `final_mean` and `final_var` into shared memory, so all threads get the right value. Actually, `group_acc` is defined inside the lambda and updated in thread 0 only within the loop. That should be fine because after the loop, thread 0 writes to shared memory. Hmm, but `welford_combine(group_acc, block_res)` in thread 0 while `group_acc` for other threads doesn't get updated - that's fine because they don't use it. Wait, there's a subtle issue: in the loop, `group_acc = welford_combine(group_acc, block_res)` runs only when `threadIdx.x == 0`. But then we have `__syncthreads()` which ensures all threads wait until thread 0 is done. Then the next iteration of the loop starts. That's fine. Shared memory size: `chan_means` needs up to `cpg` floats. If cpg can be up to e.g. 16, we can declare extern shared with enough space. But we have `Welford warp_buf[32]` which is 32*3*4 bytes = 384 bytes, plus some other floats. The `chan_means` array as `float*` requires cpg floats. But for safety, maybe we should allocate it as `float chan_means[16]` in shared memory if we know max. Actually, since `extern __shared__ float sh[]` is dynamic, we can pass the size at kernel launch. The size would be `cpg * sizeof(float) + 32 * sizeof(Welford) + ...`. But `Welford` contains 3 floats, so 384 bytes for warp_buf. Plus cpg floats for means. Plus a few more. Total < 1KB. It's cleaner to just request a fixed amount like `1024` bytes. Wait, but `chan_means` is declared as `float* chan_means = sh;` and then we index `chan_means[rc]`. This is fine. One issue: in the kernel I wrote `Welford group_acc = {0, 0, 0};` at the top. But in the loop, only thread 0 updates it. Other threads' `group_acc` is unused. That's fine. But actually, we should be careful: `group_acc` for thread 0 is the combination of all channels. After the loop, it writes to shared memory. All good. Actually, I think there's a performance concern: Welford's online update in the inner loop might use more operations than simple sum+sq. But it only adds a few FMAs per element. With the kernel being memory-bound, it shouldn't matter. However, looking at the inner loop, `acc.n += 1.0f` means we're doing a division `delta / acc.n` every iteration. The compiler might optimize `1.0f / acc.n` with reciprocal, but still. For 43 iterations per thread per channel, it's fine. Alternatively, since all subsets have the same size (either floor(spatial_size/blockDim.x) or ceil), we could use a simpler approach. But with grid-stride loops, the last threads might process one more element. Hmm, actually with grid-stride, `spatial_size = 10933`, `blockDim.x = 256`: - Threads 0 to 10879 mod 256 = threads 0 to 127: process 43 elements - Threads 128 to 255: process 42 elements So there's a slight imbalance. But Welford handles different n naturally. Actually, the Welford combine properly handles different `n` values, so it's correct even with differing subset sizes. Let me verify the math with mean pooling: - The mean of `normalized` over spatial is `mean(normalized) = mean((x - group_mean) / std) = (mean(x) - group_mean) / std = (chan_mean - group_mean) / std`. - Since affine is applied after normalization: `affine(normalized) = normalized * gamma + beta` - Mean over spatial: `mean(affine(normalized)) = mean(normalized) * gamma + beta = ((chan_mean - group_mean) / std) * gamma + beta`. Yes! This is correct. Now, what about `m2` for empty subsets? If a thread processes 0 elements, `acc.n = 0`. In Welford combine, if `r.n == 0`, we'd have issues with division. But `a.n = 0, b.n > 0`: `delta = b.mean - 0`, `r.mean = 0 + delta * b.n / b.n = b.mean`, `r.m2 = 0 + b.m2 + delta * delta * 0 * b.n / b.n = b.m2`. So it works! But we should verify with floating point. Actually, if `a.n = 0` and `b.n > 0`: - `r.n = b.n` - `delta = b.mean - a.mean` = `b.mean - 0` = `b.mean` - `r.mean = 0 + b.mean * b.n / b.n = b.mean` ✓ - `r.m2 = 0 + b.m2 + b.mean * b.mean * 0 * b.n / b.n = b.m2` ✓ If both are 0: - `r.n = 0` - Could get NaN from `b.n / r.n` if we don't check. We should handle `r.n == 0`. So add: ```cpp if (r.n == 0.0f) { r.mean = 0.0f; r.m2 = 0.0f; return r; } ``` Let me write a cleaner version of the kernel without lambdas, since CUDA device lambdas can have issues or require specific flags. ```cpp struct WelfordAcc { float n; float mean; float m2; }; __device__ inline WelfordAcc welford_combine(WelfordAcc a, WelfordAcc b) { WelfordAcc res; res.n = a.n + b.n; if (res.n == 0.0f) { res.mean = 0.0f; res.m2 = 0.0f; return res; } float delta = b.mean - a.mean; res.mean = a.mean + delta * b.n / res.n; res.m2 = a.m2 + b.m2 + delta * delta * a.n * b.n / res.n; return res; } __device__ inline WelfordAcc warp_reduce_welford(WelfordAcc val) { for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { WelfordAcc other; other.n = __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val.n, offset); other.mean = __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val.mean, offset); other.m2 = __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val.m2, offset); val = welford_combine(val, other); } return val; } __global__ void fused_hardswish_gn_mean_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ gamma, const float* __restrict__ beta, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C, int D, int H, int W, int num_groups, float eps) { extern __shared__ float s[]; float* channel_means = s; WelfordAcc* warp_buf = (WelfordAcc*)(s + 32); // enough space float* group_mean_ptr = (float*)(warp_buf + 32); float* group_var_ptr = group_mean_ptr + 1; int b = blockIdx.x; int g = blockIdx.y; int cpg = C / num_groups; int c0 = g * cpg; int spatial_size = D * H * W; WelfordAcc group_acc; group_acc.n = 0.0f; group_acc.mean = 0.0f; group_acc.m2 = 0.0f; for (int rc = 0; rc < cpg; ++rc) { int c = c0 + rc; int base = (b * C + c) * spatial_size; WelfordAcc acc; acc.n = 0.0f; acc.mean = 0.0f; acc.m2 = 0.0f; for (int s_idx = threadIdx.x; s_idx < spatial_size; s_idx += blockDim.x) { float x = __ldg(input + base + s_idx); x = x * fminf(fmaxf(x + 3.0f, 0.0f), 6.0f) / 6.0f; acc.n += 1.0f; float delta = x - acc.mean; acc.mean += delta / acc.n; float delta2 = x - acc.mean; acc.m2 += delta * delta2; } WelfordAcc lane_res = warp_reduce_welford(acc); int lane = threadIdx.x & 31; int wid = threadIdx.x >> 5; if (lane == 0) { warp_buf[wid] = lane_res; } __syncthreads(); WelfordAcc block_res; block_res.n = 0.0f; block_res.mean = 0.0f; block_res.m2 = 0.0f; int num_warps = (blockDim.x + 31) >> 5; if (threadIdx.x < num_warps) { block_res = warp_buf[threadIdx.x]; block_res = warp_reduce_welford(block_res); } if (threadIdx.x == 0) { channel_means[rc] = block_res.mean; group_acc = welford_combine(group_acc, block_res); } __syncthreads(); } if (threadIdx.x == 0) { *group_mean_ptr = group_acc.mean; *group_var_ptr = (group_acc.n > 0.0f) ? (group_acc.m2 / group_acc.n) : 0.0f; } __syncthreads(); float mean = *group_mean_ptr; float var = fmaxf(*group_var_ptr, 0.0f); float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); float spatial_inv = 1.0f / float(spatial_size); if (threadIdx.x < cpg) { int c = c0 + threadIdx.x; float cmean = channel_means[threadIdx.x]; float out_val = ((cmean - mean) * inv_std) * gamma[c] + beta[c]; output[b * C + c] = out_val; } } ``` Shared memory calculation: - channel_means: 32 floats max - warp_buf: 32 WelfordAcc = 32 * 12 bytes = 384 bytes - group_mean_ptr + group_var_ptr: 8 bytes Total: 32*4 + 384 + 8 = 136 + 384 + 8 = 528 bytes. Round to 1024 for safety. Grid: (B, num_groups) Block: 128 or 256 threads. Let's use 256 for fewer iterations.
