The conv is done by cuDNN and writes output to NCHW format. The input to our kernel is the conv output. Access pattern for our kernel within a channel is contiguous along the W dimension (stride 1), then H (stride W), then D (stride H*W). For a grid-stride loop `s = threadIdx.x; s < spatial_size; s += blockDim.x`, the first 29 values for thread 0 are W=0..28, then thread 1 gets W=0..28 offset by one... wait no. Actually `s` is the flat spatial index. In NCHW, the memory layout for channel c is contiguous in spatial dimensions. So `input[base + 0], input[base + 1], ... input[base + spatial_size-1]` are all contiguous. With threadIdx.x = 0 reading base+0, base+256, base+512... threadIdx.x = 1 reading base+1, base+257, base+513... All these are contiguous! The warp reads 256 contiguous floats starting from `base + 0`, then `base + 256`, etc. This is perfectly coalesced. But is it? For a warp of 32 threads: - Thread 0 reads base + 0 - Thread 1 reads base + 1 - ... - Thread 31 reads base + 31 These are 32 contiguous floats. Coalesced! Next iteration: base+256 to base+287. Also coalesced. What about when we use blockDim.x = 256 and have multiple warps? - Warp 0: threads 0-31 reading base+0 to base+31 - Warp 1: threads 32-63 reading base+32 to base+63 - Warp 2: base+64 to base+95 - Warp 3: base+96 to base+127 - Warp 4: base+128 to base+159 - Warp 5: base+160 to base+191 - Warp 6: base+192 to base+223 - Warp 7: base+224 to base+255 All warps read contiguous segments. This is perfectly coalesced AND cache-friendly (adjacent warps access adjacent cache lines). Next iteration: base+256 to base+511. Same pattern. This is basically the ideal memory access pattern. Now, for `__ldg`, it would use the read-only data cache. Since all threads in a block access the same channel consecutively, L2 cache should work well. L1 should also help within a warp. Given this excellent memory access pattern, the kernel should run at close to memory bandwidth. Let me estimate performance: - Total reads: 174.8M floats = 699 MB - Memory bandwidth: 3.35 TB/s - Minimum kernel time from reads: 699 / 3.35e12 = 0.208 ms - With compute overhead: maybe 0.3-0.4 ms The conv in cuDNN: input size = 1024*3*16*32*32 = 50,331,648 floats = ~201 MB. Weights negligible. Output write = 699 MB. Total conv memory: ~900 MB. At 3.35 TB/s, 0.27 ms minimum. But conv is compute bound due to the many FMAs. Let's say conv takes 0.8-1.2ms. Total forward time: ~1.2ms (conv) + ~0.4ms (fused) = ~1.6ms. Reference time: conv ~1.2ms + hardswish ~0.2ms + group norm ~0.4ms + mean ~0.1ms = ~1.9ms. Plus kernel launch overhead and memory round trips. Maybe 2.0-2.5ms. Our speedup: ~1.25x to 1.5x. Not huge but decent. Can we do more? What if we also fuse the hardswish computation into the conv forward? We could use a custom conv kernel that computes and directly reduces, eliminating the need to write and read 699 MB intermediate. Custom direct conv kernel with integrated reduction: - For each (b, c), compute conv3d on the fly and accumulate spatial mean and sum_sq. - This saves 699 MB write + 699 MB read = ~1.4 GB. - At 3.35 TB/s, that's 0.42ms saved. - Plus we avoid the kernel boundary. For the conv itself, we're still doing the compute. But if we use a naive direct conv without shared memory input caching, the memory bandwidth for reading input is: - Input: 1024*3*16*32*32 = 50M floats = 201 MB (read once if cached well) But without caching, we'd read 174.8M outputs * 192 input floats each = 33.6B floats = 134 GB! That's terrible. Can we get some caching? If a block handles one (b, c), and processes all spatial positions with 256 threads: - Thread 0 reads input patch at (0,0,0): input[b, 0:3, 0:4, 0:4, 0:4] - Thread 1 reads input patch at (0,0,1): input[b, 0:3, 0:4, 0:4, 1:5] These overlap heavily in the W dimension. With L1 cache, patches share many values. But L1 cache is only 128KB per SM. A single patch is 3*4*4*4 = 192 floats = 768 bytes. 256 patches = 192KB. That exceeds L1. But consecutive threads access overlapping regions, so cache lines are reused between threads in a warp or neighboring warps. For positions (d, h, w) and (d, h, w+1), they share 3*4*4*3 = 144 floats out of 192. That's 75% overlap! For positions (d, h, w) and (d, h, w+32), the overlap in W is 4-32 = negative, so no overlap! The grid-stride loop means thread 0 and thread 32 read patches starting at w=0 and w=32. Since W=29, thread 32 is reading beyond the dimension. Actually W=29 is the output width, but input width is 32. Output w ranges from 0 to 28. Thread 32 will read output position w=32, which doesn't exist. So blockDim.x=256 > spatial_size iterations means the stride is large. Wait, spatial_size = 10933. BlockDim.x = 256. Thread 0 reads s = 0, 256, 512, 768, ... Thread 1 reads s = 1, 257, 513, 769, ... In terms of W coordinate (s mod 29): Thread 0: w = 0, then w = 256 mod 29 = 256 - 232 = 24, then 512 mod 29 = 512 - 493 = 19, etc. These are far apart in W. The input patches at w=0 and w=24 don't overlap at all in W (w=0 uses W=0..3, w=24 uses W=24..27). No overlap! So with grid-stride 256 in the flat spatial index, consecutive accesses by the same thread are NOT spatially adjacent. The memory access pattern is still contiguous at each step (all 256 threads read 256 contiguous elements), but each element corresponds to a completely different spatial position, so the input patches needed for the conv at those positions are scattered. This means L1/L2 cache reuse is terrible for loading input patches. The direct conv approach with this thread layout would be extremely memory bound and inefficient. To fix this, we'd need to process spatially adjacent output positions. E.g., tile the output spatially and have threads process nearby positions. But this complicates the reduction. So using cuDNN for the conv is much better because cuDNN has sophisticated algorithms (implicit GEMM, Winograd, FFT, etc.) that are highly optimized for 3D conv. The pragmatic approach stands: use cuDNN conv + fused reduction kernel. One more thought: can we use torch's `torch.compile` to get automatic fusion? The instructions say to use a custom CUDA kernel. So we can't just rely on compile. But we can use the conv from PyTorch and our kernel for the rest. Let me now think about improving the fused kernel. Instead of 4096 blocks of 256 threads, which means processing 4 channels per block, what about using 16384 blocks of 128 threads, one per (b, c)? For the 16384-block approach: - Block handles one channel. - All 128 threads reduce 10933 elements. - Writes channel sum and sum_sq to global memory. - Second kernel: 4096 blocks (one per b,g), each reads 4 channel sums, computes final. Kernel 1: simple, lots of parallelism. Kernel 2: very simple, minimal work. Let me compare complexity: - Kernel 1 has no group-level logic, just elementwise(hardswish) + reduction. - Kernel 2 has simple arithmetic. This is arguably SIMPLER and might have better occupancy. Let me think about the kernel 2 if we use the two-kernel approach. Two-kernel approach: Kernel 1: `hardswish_reduce_spatial` - Input: (B, C, D, H, W) - Output: `mean` array (B, C) and `m2` array (B, C)? Or just sum and sum_sq. - Actually we need `sum` and `sum_sq` (or M2). - Or we can output mean and m2 (variance). Wait, for Welford across the group, we need (n, mean, M2) for each channel. Since n is constant (spatial_size), we just need mean and M2 per channel. So kernel 1 writes: - `channel_means` (B, C) floats - `channel_m2` (B, C) floats Kernel 2 reads these, combines channels in each group using Welford, and outputs final (B, C). Kernel 1: each block handles one (b, c). ```cpp __global__ void kernel1(const float* input, float* means, float* m2s, int B, int C, int spatial_size) { int b = blockIdx.x; int c = blockIdx.y; int base = (b * C + c) * spatial_size; WelfordAcc acc = {0, 0, 0}; for (int s = threadIdx.x; s < spatial_size; s += blockDim.x) { float x = __ldg(input + base + s); x = x * fminf(fmaxf(x + 3.0f, 0.0f), 6.0f) / 6.0f; acc.n += 1.0f; float delta = x - acc.mean; acc.mean += delta / acc.n; float delta2 = x - acc.mean; acc.m2 += delta * delta2; } // block reduce // ... if (threadIdx.x == 0) { means[b * C + c] = acc.mean; m2s[b * C + c] = acc.m2; } } ``` Wait, we need to reduce `acc` across the block. Same reduction as before. With 16384 blocks of 128 threads: - 128 threads per block, 16384 blocks. - Concurrent blocks per SM: 2048/128 = 16. 132*16 = 2112 concurrent. - Waves: 16384/2112 = ~7.8 waves. - Total memory read: 699 MB. 0.21ms at peak BW. - Plus writes: 2 * 16384 * 4 = 131KB. Negligible. - Kernel time: ~0.25-0.4ms. Kernel 2: one block per (b, g) or one thread per (b, c). Let's do one block per (b, g) with 4 threads (one per channel). ```cpp __global__ void kernel2(const float* means, const float* m2s, const float* gamma, const float* beta, float* output, int B, int C, int cpg, float eps) { int b = blockIdx.x; int g = blockIdx.y; int c0 = g * cpg; // Welford combine all channels in group WelfordAcc group = {0, 0, 0}; for (int rc = 0; rc < cpg; rc++) { int c = c0 + rc; int idx = b * C + c; WelfordAcc ch = {spatial_size, means[idx], m2s[idx]}; // Wait, m2 needs to be combined properly // For Welford, we need both n and m2. If we have n=spatial_size for each channel. group = welford_combine(group, ch); } float mean = group.mean; float var = group.m2 / group.n; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); if (threadIdx.x < cpg) { int c = c0 + threadIdx.x; float cmean = means[b * C + c]; output[b * C + c] = ((cmean - mean) * inv_std) * gamma[c] + beta[c]; } } ``` This kernel 2 is tiny. 4096 blocks of 4 threads. It will run in ~0.01ms. Total time: ~0.35ms for kernel 1 + conv time. Same as the single-kernel approach but arguably simpler. Actually, let me check: does Welford combine work when we pass in `m2` from a completed Welford with `n = spatial_size`? Yes. If a single Welford run processed `spatial_size` elements and ended with `(spatial_size, mean, m2)`, then combining two such channels is exactly what we need. But there's a catch: in kernel 1, the per-thread Welford acc has `n` equal to the number of elements processed by that thread, not `spatial_size`. After block reduction, the resulting Welford acc should have `n = spatial_size`. The reduction with Welford naturally preserves this. So in kernel 1, after block reduction, `block_res.n` will equal `spatial_size`. We write `block_res.mean` and `block_res.m2` to global memory. In kernel 2, we reconstruct `WelfordAcc ch = {spatial_size, means[idx], m2s[idx]}` and combine. This is correct! Now, which is better: single kernel or two kernels? The two-kernel approach has: - More blocks in kernel 1 (16384 vs 4096), which might better saturate the GPU in the first phase. - But requires an extra global memory write of ~131KB and read of ~131KB in kernel 2. This is negligible. - Slightly more kernel launch overhead (2 launches instead of 1). I think the single-kernel approach is fine and likely as fast or faster due to avoiding the second kernel launch and the intermediate memory. But the two-kernel approach might have better occupancy in kernel 1. Actually, let me reconsider the single-kernel shared memory consumption. I used `extern __shared__` with manual pointer management. In the single kernel with 256 threads and cpg channels processed sequentially, each channel requires a block reduction. The shared memory usage includes `warp_buf[32]` for Welford (384 bytes) and `channel_means[cpg]` and some pointers. With 256 threads per block and shared memory usage ~500 bytes, occupancy is limited by threads (256*8 = 2048 max) and probably not by shared memory. Register count might be the main limit. Let me try both approaches. I'll start with the single-kernel approach because it avoids inter-kernel memory. If there's correctness or performance issues, I can switch. Wait, actually the fused single kernel I designed processes channels sequentially in a loop. This means all 256 threads work on channel 0 together, then channel 1, etc. But in NCHW, channel 0 and channel 1 are `spatial_size` apart in memory. After finishing channel 0, the cache that held channel 0 is cold for channel 1. But channel 1 is a completely different memory region anyway. Alternative: interleave channel processing. Process all channels in one pass over the spatial dimension. ```cpp for (int s = threadIdx.x; s < spatial_size; s += blockDim.x) { for (int rc = 0; rc < cpg; rc++) { int c = c0 + rc; float x = __ldg(input + (b * C + c) * spatial_size + s); // process } } ``` This keeps reads for the same spatial position across channels close together in time. But in NCHW, x[b, c0, s] and x[b, c0+1, s] are `spatial_size` apart, so they likely map to different cache lines. The read for x[b, c0+1, s] might not be in L1/L2 cache even though we just read x[b, c0, s]. Actually, the L2 cache is large (50MB on H100). The total conv output is 700MB, which exceeds L2. But a single block only touches 4 * 10933 * 4 = 175KB. Many such blocks can fit in L2. So sequential vs interleaved might not matter much. I think sequential channel processing is fine and matches the memory stride pattern better (contiguous within a channel). Let me finalize the single-kernel design. I'll write the CUDA code. One more thing: PyTorch's default memory allocator might not guarantee that tensor allocations start at aligned addresses for float4. But for float reads, alignment to 4 bytes is guaranteed since `float` is 4 bytes and tensor elements are contiguous. Now I also need to figure out how to call `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline`. Let me sketch the Python code: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline # Make sure to add torch include paths cuda_source = """ #include <cmath> #include <cuda_runtime.h> struct WelfordAcc { float n; float mean; float m2; }; __device__ inline WelfordAcc welford_combine(WelfordAcc a, WelfordAcc b) { WelfordAcc res; res.n = a.n + b.n; if (res.n == 0.0f) { res.mean = 0.0f; res.m2 = 0.0f; return res; } float delta = b.mean - a.mean; res.mean = a.mean + delta * b.n / res.n; res.m2 = a.m2 + b.m2 + delta * delta * a.n * b.n / res.n; return res; } __device__ inline WelfordAcc warp_reduce_welford(WelfordAcc val) { for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { WelfordAcc other; other.n = __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val.n, offset); other.mean = __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val.mean, offset); other.m2 = __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val.m2, offset); val = welford_combine(val, other); } return val; } __global__ void fused_hardswish_gn_mean_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ gamma, const float* __restrict__ beta, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C, int D, int H, int W, int num_groups, float eps) { extern __shared__ float s[]; float* channel_means = s; WelfordAcc* warp_buf = (WelfordAcc*)(s + 32); float* group_mean_ptr = (float*)(warp_buf + 32); float* group_var_ptr = group_mean_ptr + 1; int b = blockIdx.x; int g = blockIdx.y; int cpg = C / num_groups; int c0 = g * cpg; int spatial_size = D * H * W; WelfordAcc group_acc; group_acc.n = 0.0f; group_acc.mean = 0.0f; group_acc.m2 = 0.0f; for (int rc = 0; rc < cpg; ++rc) { int c = c0 + rc; int base = (b * C + c) * spatial_size; WelfordAcc acc; acc.n = 0.0f; acc.mean = 0.0f; acc.m2 = 0.0f; for (int s_idx = threadIdx.x; s_idx < spatial_size; s_idx += blockDim.x) { float x = __ldg(input + base + s_idx); x = x * fminf(fmaxf(x + 3.0f, 0.0f), 6.0f) / 6.0f; acc.n += 1.0f; float delta = x - acc.mean; acc.mean += delta / acc.n; float delta2 = x - acc.mean; acc.m2 += delta * delta2; } WelfordAcc lane_res = warp_reduce_welford(acc); int lane = threadIdx.x & 31; int wid = threadIdx.x >> 5; if (lane == 0) { warp_buf[wid] = lane_res; } __syncthreads(); WelfordAcc block_res; block_res.n = 0.0f; block_res.mean = 0.0f; block_res.m2 = 0.0f; int num_warps = (blockDim.x + 31) >> 5; if (threadIdx.x < num_warps) { block_res = warp_buf[threadIdx.x]; block_res = warp_reduce_welford(block_res); } if (threadIdx.x == 0) { channel_means[rc] = block_res.mean; group_acc = welford_combine(group_acc, block_res); } __syncthreads(); } if (threadIdx.x == 0) { *group_mean_ptr = group_acc.mean; *group_var_ptr = (group_acc.n > 0.0f) ? (group_acc.m2 / group_acc.n) : 0.0f; } __syncthreads(); float mean = *group_mean_ptr; float var = fmaxf(*group_var_ptr, 0.0f); float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); if (threadIdx.x < cpg) { int c = c0 + threadIdx.x; float cmean = channel_means[threadIdx.x]; float out_val = ((cmean - mean) * inv_std) * gamma[c] + beta[c]; output[b * C + c] = out_val; } } """ cpp_source = """ void fused_hardswish_gn_mean( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor gamma, torch::Tensor beta, torch::Tensor output, int num_groups, float eps) { int B = input.size(0); int C = input.size(1); int D = input.size(2); int H = input.size(3); int W = input.size(4); dim3 grid(B, num_groups); dim3 block(256); int shared_mem = 1024; // enough for our needs fused_hardswish_gn_mean_kernel<<<grid, block, shared_mem>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), gamma.data_ptr<float>(), beta.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), B, C, D, H, W, num_groups, eps); } """ ``` Wait, I need to make sure the block reduction properly handles the case when `blockDim.x` is larger than 32 but the number of warps is more than 32. Here blockDim.x = 256, so num_warps = 8. That's less than 32, so `warp_buf[32]` and the second warp reduce work fine. What if someone uses blockDim.x = 1024? Then num_warps = 32. The second reduction: `if (threadIdx.x < num_warps)` means threads 0..31 participate, each reads one warp result, then they do a warp reduce. The `warp_reduce_welford` among 32 threads works correctly because all 32 participate. Good. What about blockDim.x = 512? num_warps = 16. Threads 0..15 do the second reduction. `warp_reduce_welford` involves `__shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, ...)` among all 32 threads in the warp, but only threads 0..15 call it. The shuffle is a warp-level instruction that executes for all threads in the warp regardless. If inactive threads have undefined values, the shuffle gets garbage. Warp 0 has threads 0..31. If num_warps <= 32, all threads in warp 0 participate with valid data. If num_warps > 32... well, we have warp_buf size 32, and 32 is the maximum meaningful warp reduce size anyway. If num_warps < 32, threads num_warps..31 initialized to zero, and Welford combine with zero works fine. The warp shuffle only communicates within a warp, and all threads in warp 0 are in the same warp. But `warp_buf` was written with `__syncthreads()` before the read. Let's trace: 1. Threads write to `warp_buf[wid]` (after warp reduce). 2. `__syncthreads()` ensures all writes are visible. 3. All threads read `warp_buf[threadIdx.x]` (or zero if out of range). 4. Let me write the full kernel with proper float casts for eps, and make sure `fmaxf`, `fminf`, `rsqrtf` are used correctly. Actually, one more issue: Welford combine with zero n. Let me test it mentally: a = (0, 0, 0), b = (n2, mean2, m2_2) res.n = n2 delta = mean2 - 0 = mean2 res.mean = 0 + mean2 * n2 / n2 = mean2 res.m2 = 0 + m2_2 + mean2 * mean2 * 0 * n2 / n2 = m2_2 Works. Now, let me also verify the mean pooling formula: PyTorch group norm does: `y = (x - mean) / sqrt(var + eps) * weight + bias` Where mean and var are computed over the group dimensions (all channels in group and spatial). Mean pooling: `out = mean(y, dim=[2,3,4])` For a specific channel c in group g: `mean(y[b, c, :, :, :]) = mean((x[b, c, :, :, :] - group_mean) / sqrt(group_var + eps) * gamma[c] + beta[c])` The group_mean and group_var and gamma, beta are constant over the spatial dimension. So: `= ((mean(x[b, c, :, :, :]) - group_mean) / sqrt(group_var + eps)) * gamma[c] + beta[c]` `= ((channel_mean - group_mean) * inv_std) * gamma[c] + beta[c]` Yes, this is correct! One more thing: PyTorch's group norm computes the biased variance (divides by N, not N-1). Welford's `m2 / n` gives the biased variance. Good. Also, PyTorch's default eps for GroupNorm is 1e-5. Let me now write the complete `ModelNew` class with inline extension loading. Actually, I should think about whether the overhead of `load_inline` is acceptable. It compiles at module load time, which happens once. That's fine. One optimization: since `self.conv` and `self.group_norm` are the same layers as in `Model`, we can initialize them the same way. But we need to make sure our `ModelNew` accepts the same args and sets things up the same. ```python class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, num_groups=4, bias=True): super(ModelNew, self).__init__() self.conv = nn.Conv3d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, bias=bias) self.group_norm = nn.GroupNorm(num_groups, out_channels) self._load_extension() ``` Wait, `_load_extension` should ideally be done once at module level, not per instance. But since we might instantiate multiple times (unlikely in the benchmark), we can load it lazily as a module-level variable. One slight concern: in my Welford combine, when `res.n` is large, `delta * delta * a.n * b.n / res.n` could overflow? For typical float values and n~40000, `a.n * b.n / res.n` is at most ~20000. Block 0 (g=0, c=0): - Loop rc=0: base = 0*4+0)*8 = 0. - Each thread processes s_idx = threadIdx.x, threadIdx.x+256, ... but spatial_size=8, so only threadIdx.x < 8 process elements. - Acc for thread 0: processes x[0..7]? No, thread 0 processes s_idx=0 only. Thread 1 processes s_idx=1. Thread 7 processes s_idx=7. Thread 8+ processes nothing (acc stays zero). - Warp reduce combines threads 0..31. Thread 0 gets combined result of threads 0..7 (valid) and threads 8..31 (zero). The combination will correctly ignore zeros because those threads have acc.n=0. - Wait, threads 8..31 have acc = {0, 0, 0}. When we warp reduce, thread 0 combines with thread 1 (valid), then thread 2 (valid)... up to thread 7. Then with thread 8 (zero). Welford combine with zero works correctly (returns the non-zero result). - After warp reduce, lane_res for lane 0 has n=8, correct mean, correct m2. - Thread 0 writes to warp_buf[0]. - All threads: block_res = warp_buf[0] for thread 0, zero for others. - `if (threadIdx.x < 32)`: threads 0..31 all have block_res = warp_buf[threadIdx.x] for thread 0, zero for others. - Warp reduce: thread 0 starts with warp_buf[0], thread 1 starts with 0, etc. - After reduce, thread 0 has combined result = correct block_res. - Thread 0 writes channel_means[0] = mean, group_acc = welford_combine((0,0,0), block_res) = block_res. - After loop, group_mean = block_res.mean, group_var = block_res.m2 / 8. - Thread 0 writes output. Block (b=0, g=0): handles c=0,1,2,3. - Loop rc=0 (c=0): block reduce -> channel_means[0] = mean_0, group_acc = (10933, mean_0, m2_0) - Loop rc=1 (c=1): block reduce -> channel_means[1] = mean_1, group_acc = welford_combine((10933, mean_0, m2_0), (10933, mean_1, m2_1)) - ... - After loop: group_acc.n = 43732, group_acc.mean = mean over all 4 channels and spatial, group_acc.m2 = sum of squared deviations. - group_var = m2 / 43732. Correct. - Threads 0..3 write output for channels 0..3 using channel_means[0..3]. Numerical equivalence: PyTorch group norm uses Welford for numerical stability. Our kernel also uses Welford. The mean pooling is exact because it's a mathematical identity. So results should match very closely. One potential issue: PyTorch's hardswish might be implemented with specific rounding behavior. Our implementation: `x * min(max(x + 3, 0), 6) / 6`. PyTorch's hardswish is: `x * clamp(x + 3, 0, 6) / 6`. In float, this is identical. `fminf` and `fmaxf` are the correct CUDA equivalents. Wait, PyTorch's hardswish might have a different backward, but we only care about forward. The forward is `x * hardtanh(x+3, 0, 6) / 6`. Our formula matches. Another potential issue: for large tensor sizes, the sum in Welford might accumulate error. But Welford is specifically designed to be stable, and PyTorch uses it too. callcompile_kernel { "kernel_code": "import torch\nimport torch.nn as nn\nimport torch.nn.functional as F\nfrom torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline\n\n_cuda_source = r'''\n#include <cuda_runtime.h>\n#include <cmath>\n\nstruct WelfordAcc {\n float n;\n float mean;\n float m2;\n};\n\n__device__ inline WelfordAcc welford_combine(WelfordAcc a, WelfordAcc b) {\n WelfordAcc res;\n res.n = a.n + b.n;\n if (res.n == 0.0f) {\n res.mean = 0.0f;\n res.m2 = 0.0f;\n return res;\n }\n float delta = b.mean - a.mean;\n res.mean = a.mean + delta * b.n / res.n;\n res.m2 = a.m2 + b.m2 + delta * delta * a.n * b.n / res.n;\n return res;\n}\n\n__device__ inline WelfordAcc warp_reduce_welford(WelfordAcc val) {\n for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) {\n WelfordAcc other;\n other.n = __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val.n, offset);\n other.mean = __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val.mean, offset);\n other.m2 = __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val.m2, offset);\n val = welford_combine(val, other);\n }\n return val;\n}\n\n__global__ void fused_hardswish_gn_mean_kernel(\n const float* __restrict__ input,\n const float* __restrict__ gamma,\n const float* __restrict__ beta,\n float* __restrict__ output,\n int B, int C, int D, int H, int W, int num_groups, float eps) {\n\n extern __shared__ float s[];\n float* channel_means = s;\n WelfordAcc* warp_buf = (WelfordAcc*)(s + 32);\n float* group_mean_ptr = (float*)(warp_buf + 32);\n float* group_var_ptr = group_mean_ptr + 1;\n\n int b = blockIdx.x;\n int g = blockIdx.y;\n int cpg = C / num_groups;\n int c0 = g * cpg;\n int spatial_size = D * H * W;\n\n WelfordAcc group_acc;\n group_acc.n = 0.0f;\n group_acc.mean = 0.0f;\n group_acc.m2 = 0.0f;\n\n for (int rc = 0; rc < cpg; ++rc) {\n int c = c0 + rc;\n int base = (b * C + c) * spatial_size;\n\n WelfordAcc acc;\n acc.n = 0.0f;\n acc.mean = 0.0f;\n acc.m2 = 0.0f;\n\n for (int s_idx = threadIdx.x; s_idx < spatial_size; s_idx += blockDim.x) {\n float x = __ldg(input + base + s_idx);\n x = x * fminf(fmaxf(x + 3.0f, 0.0f), 6.0f) / 6.0f;\n\n acc.n += 1.0f;\n float delta = x - acc.mean;\n acc.mean += delta / acc.n;\n float delta2 = x - acc.mean;\n acc.m2 += delta * delta2;\n }\n\n WelfordAcc lane_res = warp_reduce_welford(acc);\n int lane = threadIdx.x & 31;\n int wid = threadIdx.x >> 5;\n\n if (lane == 0) {\n warp_buf[wid] = lane_res;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n int num_warps = (blockDim.x + 31) >> 5;\n WelfordAcc block_res;\n if (threadIdx.x < num_warps) {\n block_res = warp_buf[threadIdx.x];\n } else {\n block_res.n = 0.0f;\n block_res.mean = 0.0f;\n block_res.m2 = 0.0f;\n }\n\n if (threadIdx.x < 32) {\n block_res = warp_reduce_welford(block_res);\n if (threadIdx.x == 0) {\n channel_means[rc] = block_res.mean;\n group_acc = welford_combine(group_acc, block_res);\n }\n }\n __syncthreads();\n }\n\n if (threadIdx.x == 0) {\n *group_mean_ptr = group_acc.mean;\n *group_var_ptr = (group_acc.n > 0.0f) ? (group_acc.m2 / group_acc.n) : 0.0f;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n float mean = *group_mean_ptr;\n float var = fmaxf(*group_var_ptr, 0.0f);\n float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps);\n\n if (threadIdx.x < cpg) {\n int c = c0 + threadIdx.x;\n float cmean = channel_means[threadIdx.x];\n float out_val = ((cmean - mean) * inv_std) * gamma[c] + beta[c];\n output[b * C + c] = out_val;\n }\n}\n'''\n\n_cpp_source = r'''\n#include <torch/extension.h>\n\nvoid fused_forward(\n torch::Tensor input,\n torch::Tensor gamma,\n torch::Tensor beta,\n torch::Tensor output,\n int num_groups,\n float eps) {\n\n int B = input.size(0);\n int C = input.size(1);\n int D = input.size(2);\n int H = input.size(3);\n int W = input.size(4);\n\n dim3 grid(B, num_groups);\n dim3 block(256);\n int shared_mem = 2048;\n\n fused_hardswish_gn_mean_kernel<<<grid, block, shared_mem>>>(\n input.data_ptr<float>(),\n gamma.data_ptr<float>(),\n beta.data_ptr<float>(),\n output.data_ptr<float>(),\n B, C, D, H, W, num_groups, eps\n );\n}\n'''\n\n_fused_ops = None\n\ndef _get_fused_ops():\n global _fused_ops\n if _fused_ops is None:\n _fused_ops = load_inline(\n name=\"fused_hardswish_gn_mean\",\n cpp_sources=_cpp_source,\n cuda_sources=_cuda_source,\n functions=[\"fused_forward\"],\n extra_cuda_cflags=[\"-O3\", \"--use_fast_math\"],\n verbose=False,\n )\n return _fused_ops\n\n\nclass ModelNew(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, num_groups=4, bias=True):\n super(ModelNew, self).__init__()\n self.conv = nn.Conv3d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, bias=bias)\n self.group_norm = nn.GroupNorm(num_groups, out_channels)\n\n def forward(self, x):\n x = self.conv(x)\n B, C, D, H, W = x.shape\n out = torch.empty(B, C, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype)\n _get_fused_ops().fused_forward(\n x,\n self.group_norm.weight,\n self.group_norm.bias,\n out,\n self.group_norm.num_groups,\n self.group_norm.eps,\n )\n return out\n" }

The advantage of two kernels: - Each kernel is very simple with high parallelism - No cross-channel sync within a block - Kernel A has very regular memory access and arithemtic The disadvantage: - Extra global memory traffic for intermediate buffer (B*C*2*4 bytes = ~131KB, which is tiny) Given that the total intermediate is only 131KB and the kernels are tiny, a two-kernel approach could actually be competitive. But let me try a single kernel first, since it avoids the intermediate write/read. Let me redesign the single kernel with better parallelism: ```cuda __global__ void fused_kernel( const float* input, const float* gamma, const float* beta, float* output, int B, int C, int D, int H, int W, int num_groups, float eps) { int b = blockIdx.x; int g = blockIdx.y; // group index int cpg = C / num_groups; int c0 = g * cpg; int spatial_size = D * H * W; // Each thread processes a chunk of spatial elements for ALL channels in group WelfordAcc acc[4]; // up to 4 channels per group for (int rc = 0; rc < cpg; rc++) { acc[rc].n = 0; acc[rc].mean = 0; acc[rc].m2 = 0; } // Main loop: each thread loads and processes all channels at each spatial position for (int s_idx = threadIdx.x; s_idx < spatial_size; s_idx += blockDim.x) { for (int rc = 0; rc < cpg; rc++) { int c = c0 + rc; int base = (b * C + c) * spatial_size + s_idx; float x = input[base]; x = x * fminf(fmaxf(x + 3.0f, 0.0f), 6.0f) / 6.0f; // Welford update acc[rc].n += 1.0f; float delta = x - acc[rc].mean; acc[rc].mean += delta / acc[rc].n; float delta2 = x - acc[rc].mean; acc[rc].m2 += delta * delta2; } } // Now reduce across warp for each channel // This is trickier because shfl operates on one variable at a time // For 4 channels, we can do 4 parallel reductions if we have enough active warps // Actually, let's just do sequential reductions __shared__ WelfordAcc shared[32]; // 8 warps * 4 channels // For each channel... WelfordAcc block_acc[4]; for (int rc = 0; rc < cpg; rc++) { // Warp reduce WelfordAcc lane_res = warp_reduce_welford(acc[rc]); int lane = threadIdx.x & 31; int wid = threadIdx.x >> 5; if (lane == 0) { shared[g * 8 + wid] = lane_res; // Wait, this index is wrong } } } ``` Hmm, the shared memory indexing gets complex with multiple channels. Let me simplify: Shared memory layout: WelfordAcc shared[8][4] for 8 warps and 4 channels - Lane 0 of each warp writes to shared[wid][rc] for each channel rc - Then one warp reads shared[0..num_warps-1][rc] for each channel rc and reduces The issue is that within a single warp, we can only do one shfl-based reduction at a time. But we have 32 threads in a warp, and each thread can hold 4 channels. We can use warp shuffle to reduce each channel's WelfordAcc across the warp. Actually, let's think about this more carefully. A WelfordAcc has 3 floats. For warp shuffle, we need to shuffle each float separately: - shfl_down for n - shfl_down for mean - shfl_down for m2 For cpg=4 channels, that's 12 shfl operations per warp to reduce all channels. With 256 threads = 8 warps, each warp does 12 shfls... that's 96 shfls total. At 1 cycle per shfl on H100, this is very fast. But we need to do this for each rc, one at a time within the warp. So each thread does 4 * 3 = 12 shfl operations. Then after warp reduce, we store to shared memory. Each warp stores 4 WelfordAcc (48 bytes). Lane 0 of each warp does this. Then __syncthreads(). Then we need to reduce across warps. We can have one warp read all 8 * 4 WelfordAcc values from shared memory and reduce them. Or better, we can have thread 0..7 each handle one warp's 4-channel WelfordAcc, and do warp shuffle reduce. Actually, an even simpler approach: use atomic operations on shared memory... no, Welford combining isn't atomic-friendly. Let me write the simplest clean version: ```cuda __global__ void fused_kernel(...) { // ... compute acc[4] per thread ... __shared__ float s_n[8][4]; __shared__ float s_mean[8][4]; __shared__ float s_m2[8][4]; __shared__ float group_mean_s; __shared__ float group_var_s; int wid = threadIdx.x >> 5; int lane = threadIdx.x & 31; int num_warps = 8; for (int rc = 0; rc < cpg; rc++) { // Warp reduce for channel rc WelfordAcc wacc = acc[rc]; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { WelfordAcc other; other.n = __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, wacc.n, offset); other.mean = __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, wacc.mean, offset); other.m2 = __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, wacc.m2, offset); wacc = welford_combine(wacc, other); } if (lane == 0) { s_n[wid][rc] = wacc.n; s_mean[wid][rc] = wacc.mean; s_m2[wid][rc] = wacc.m2; } } __syncthreads(); // Now reduce across warps for each channel if (threadIdx.x < 32) { int tid = threadIdx.x; // We use tid to index into warps. For 8 warps, tid 0..7 handles warp 0..7 for (int rc = 0; rc < cpg; rc++) { WelfordAcc wacc; wacc.n = 0.0f; wacc.mean = 0.0f; wacc.m2 = 0.0f; for (int w = 0; w < (blockDim.x >> 5); w++) { WelfordAcc other; other.n = s_n[w][rc]; other.mean = s_mean[w][rc]; other.m2 = s_m2[w][rc]; wacc = welford_combine(wacc, other); } // Now wacc is the block-level accumulator for channel rc // Store back if (tid == 0) { s_mean[0][rc] = wacc.mean; s_m2[0][rc] = wacc.m2; } } } __syncthreads(); // Combine channel stats into group stats if (threadIdx.x == 0) { WelfordAcc group_acc; group_acc.n = 0.0f; group_acc.mean = 0.0f; group_acc.m2 = 0.0f; for (int rc = 0; rc < cpg; rc++) { WelfordAcc ch_acc; ch_acc.n = spatial_size; // or s_n[0][rc] ch_acc.mean = s_mean[0][rc]; ch_acc.m2 = s_m2[0][rc]; group_acc = welford_combine(group_acc, ch_acc); } group_mean_s = group_acc.mean; group_var_s = (group_acc.n > 0.0f) ? (group_acc.m2 / group_acc.n) : 0.0f; } __syncthreads(); float mean = group_mean_s; float var = fmaxf(group_var_s, 0.0f); float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); if (threadIdx.x < cpg) { int rc = threadIdx.x; int c = c0 + rc; float cmean = s_mean[0][rc]; float out_val = ((cmean - mean) * inv_std) * gamma[c] + beta[c]; output[b * C + c] = out_val; } } ``` Wait, I see a problem in the cross-warp reduction. Only threads 0..31 participate, and each does the full reduction over all warps. But we have multiple threads in 0..31 doing redundant work on the same data! That's fine but wasteful. A better approach: Have thread 0..(num_warps-1) each read one warp's worth of 4-channel data. Then use warp shuffle to reduce. But with only 8 warps, only the first 8 threads have data, and the rest are idle. This would take more rounds. Actually, the simplest is to have ONE thread (e.g., thread 0) do the reduction in shared memory. Since it's only 8 * 4 = 32 WelfordAcc (each is 3 floats), and you do this once per block, the overhead is tiny. ```cuda if (threadIdx.x == 0) { WelfordAcc group_acc; group_acc.n = 0; group_acc.mean = 0; group_acc.m2 = 0; for (int rc = 0; rc < cpg; rc++) { WelfordAcc ch_acc; ch_acc.n = 0; ch_acc.mean = 0; ch_acc.m2 = 0; for (int w = 0; w < num_warps; w++) { WelfordAcc tmp; tmp.n = s_n[w][rc]; tmp.mean = s_mean[w][rc]; tmp.m2 = s_m2[w][rc]; ch_acc = welford_combine(ch_acc, tmp); } // ch_acc now has block-level stats for this channel s_mean[0][rc] = ch_acc.mean; s_m2[0][rc] = ch_acc.m2; WelfordAcc ch_full; ch_full.n = ch_acc.n; ch_full.mean = ch_acc.mean; ch_full.m2 = ch_acc.m2; group_acc = welford_combine(group_acc, ch_full); } group_mean_s = group_acc.mean; group_var_s = (group_acc.n > 0.0f) ? (group_acc.m2 / group_acc.n) : 0.0f; } __syncthreads(); ``` This is much cleaner! Only thread 0 does the sequential reduction. The amount of work is tiny. Shared memory needed: - s_n: 8 * 4 = 32 floats - s_mean: 32 floats - s_m2: 32 floats - group_mean_s, group_var_s: 2 floats Total: 98 floats ≈ 400 bytes. I was using 2048 bytes, so this is way less. Actually, I need room for storing the final per-channel means too. s_mean[0][rc] = 4 floats. The rest of s_mean is used during reduction. So total is fine. One more optimization: in the main loop, instead of doing: ```cuda for (int rc = 0; rc < cpg; rc++) { int c = c0 + rc; int base = (b * C + c) * spatial_size + s_idx; float x = input[base]; ... } ``` We can compute the base offset for the group and stride per channel. Since channels are separated by spatial_size elements: - base for channel c0: (b * C + c0) * spatial_size + s_idx - base for channel c0 + rc: base + rc * spatial_size Actually, `base = (b * C + c) * spatial_size + s_idx = (b * C + c0 + rc) * spatial_size + s_idx = (b * C + c0) * spatial_size + rc * spatial_size + s_idx` So for each spatial position in the loop, the channels are at offsets: - pos0: (b * C + c0) * spatial_size + s_idx - pos1: pos0 + spatial_size - pos2: pos0 + 2 * spatial_size - pos3: pos0 + 3 * spatial_size This means adjacent channels for the same spatial position are separated by spatial_size elements, which is a large stride! It's NOT coalesced if multiple threads access adjacent channels. In my current kernel, each thread accesses one channel at a time, and adjacent threads access adjacent spatial positions. This IS coalesced because adjacent threads access input[base + s_idx], input[base + s_idx + stride]... wait. For thread t and t+1 in the same warp: - Thread t accesses input[(b*C + c) * spatial_size + s_idx] - Thread t+1 accesses input[(b*C + c) * spatial_size + s_idx + spatial_stride] If spatial_stride is 1 (because blockDim.x divides spatial_size and threads are adjacent), then adjacent threads access contiguous memory! That IS coalesced. Yes, my current access pattern is coalesced: - Thread i accesses s_idx = i, i + blockDim.x, i + 2*blockDim.x, ... - Adjacent threads access s_idx = i, i+1 at the same time (for the first iteration) So threads 0, 1, 2, 3, ... access spatial positions 0, 1, 2, 3, ... which are contiguous in memory (since spatial elements are contiguous within a channel). This is perfect coalescing! OK, the memory access pattern is already optimal. Now, the new design I proposed where each thread processes all 4 channels at each spatial position would also be coalesced, because thread i still accesses spatial position s_idx for channel c0, then s_idx for channel c0+1, etc. But this changes the memory access pattern - now each thread accesses 4 non-contiguous locations (separated by spatial_size) for each iteration. Actually, let me compare: Current: thread i does 64 loads from contiguous block for channel 0, then 64 loads from contiguous block for channel 1, etc. New: thread i does 4 loads from positions separated by spatial_size, then 4 loads from positions offset by blockDim.x. The current approach is better for coalescing because threads in a warp access adjacent addresses simultaneously. Wait, actually both are coalesced. In the "new" approach, at a given cycle, all threads in the warp access spatial position s_idx for channel c0. These are separated by spatial_size between threads... no, they're at: - thread 0: (b*C + c0) * spatial_size + s_idx - thread 1: (b*C + c0) * spatial_size + (s_idx + 1) - thread 2: (b*C + c0) * spatial_size + (s_idx + 2) - ... These ARE contiguous! So it's also coalesced. Then in the next load: - thread 0: (b*C + c0+1) * spatial_size + s_idx - thread 1: (b*C + c0+1) * spatial_size + s_idx + 1 Hmm, thread 0 accesses spatial_size away from thread 1. Actually, can the GPU coalesce strided access where each thread accesses a different memory bank? For 32 threads accessing memory at base + i * stride where stride = 16384 * sizeof(float) = 65536 bytes. With L1 cache line of 128 bytes, each thread is 64KB apart. This would require 32 cache lines, which is terrible! So the "new" approach where each thread accesses multiple channels sequentially within the spatial loop is actually BAD for coalescing. The current approach (one channel at a time, all threads accessing adjacent spatial positions) is much better. Conclusion: keep the current memory access pattern. In the current code, the warp reduce is: ```cuda for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { other.n = __shfl_down_sync(..., val.n, offset); other.mean = __shfl_down_sync(..., val.mean, offset); other.m2 = __shfl_down_sync(..., val.m2, offset); val = welford_combine(val, other); } ``` For 4 channels, we can unroll this as: ```cuda for (int rc = 0; rc < cpg; rc++) { #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { // shuffle 3 floats for acc[rc] } } ``` That's 4 * 3 * 5 = 60 shfl operations per thread (5 = log2(32) levels). For dynamic sizing, I can use dynamic shared memory: ```cuda extern __shared__ float s[]; float* s_n = s; float* s_mean = s + num_warps * cpg; float* s_m2 = s + 2 * num_warps * cpg; // etc. ``` But for registers, I still need a compile-time bound. Let me be more conservative and use max_cpg=16. With 16 * 3 = 48 registers for WelfordAcc, plus some overhead, that's very safe. And cpg=4 for the test. Actually, `hardswish(x) = x * relu6(x + 3) / 6`. So `x * min(max(x + 3, 0), 6) / 6`. Let me also check if using `__fmaf_rn` or other intrinsics helps. For the Welford accumulation, the sequence is: ```cuda acc.n += 1.0f; float delta = x - acc.mean; acc.mean += delta / acc.n; float delta2 = x - acc.mean; acc.m2 += delta * delta2; ``` This is fairly optimized already. The division by acc.n could be replaced with multiplication by rcp, but the compiler might already do that. Actually, I can restructure slightly: ```cuda float n_inv = 1.0f / acc.n; float delta = x - acc.mean; acc.mean += delta * n_inv; acc.m2 += delta * (x - acc.mean); ``` This saves the `delta2` computation. But the compiler might optimize this anyway. For performance, the most important thing is to have the loop unrolled and have as few sync points as possible. My new design has only 2 syncs vs 8 in the old design. That's a big win. Let me write the final kernel code now. I also want to handle the launch configuration better. Currently I use block=256. With spatial_size=16384 and cpg=4, each thread processes 64 elements per channel, or 256 total elements across all channels. That's reasonable. If I reduce block size to 128, each thread does 128 elements, which might be better for register pressure but worse parallelism. I'll stick with 256. Wait, another thought: if I increase block size to 512, each thread does 32 elements. That might underutilize the threads but give more blocks... Actually no, with grid=(1024, 4) = 4096 blocks, we already have plenty of parallelism. The question is occupancy. With 256 threads per block, each block needs sufficient registers and shared memory. If register usage is low (say, 64 registers per thread), then 256 * 64 = 16384 registers per block. Each SM has 64K registers, so it can hold up to 4 blocks. With 4096 blocks, that's 4096 / 4 = 1024 waves across all SMs. On H100 with 132 SMs, each SM gets about 31 blocks. That's fine. If I use 128 threads per block with the same register count, each SM can hold 8 blocks, so the same total blocks are divided into 32 blocks per SM. Slightly better occupancy. But the kernel is memory-bound (loading 65536 float elements per block = 256KB), and memory bandwidth is the bottleneck. So occupancy doesn't matter as much as memory throughput. Actually, with this being memory-bound, the key is to maximize memory bandwidth utilization and minimize latency. The 256-thread block with 2 syncs should be efficient. Let me write the code. Hmm, one more thought on numerical precision. The reference PyTorch implementation might be using a specific algorithm for GroupNorm. Let me verify my understanding: PyTorch's GroupNorm forward: 1. Computes mean and variance over axes (2, 3, 4) for each group 2. Normalizes using these group statistics 3. Applies affine transform per channel The hardswish is applied element-wise before GroupNorm. My kernel computes all of this correctly. Wait, there's one subtlety. PyTorch's native_group_norm kernel uses the `welford_mean_var` or similar approach in their C++ implementation. The native implementation might use one-pass or two-pass algorithms depending on the backend. For CUDA, PyTorch uses cuDNN or their own CUDA kernel. The CUDA kernel typically uses a parallel reduction for mean and variance. Let me verify my Welford combine is correct. From the original Welford's algorithm: Given two sets A and B with means mu_A, mu_B and M2_A, M2_B: n = n_A + n_B delta = mu_B - mu_A mu = mu_A + delta * n_B / n M2 = M2_A + M2_B + delta^2 * n_A * n_B / n My welford_combine implements this exactly. Good. OK, I'm confident in the math. Let me write the code now. Let me use this approach. Also, for the per-thread WelfordAcc accumulators, I'll declare them as: ```cuda WelfordAcc acc[16]; // Initialize only the first cpg ``` Since cpg is a runtime variable, the compiler might not unroll the initialization or main loop. callcompile_kernel { "kernel_code": "import torch\nimport torch.nn as nn\nimport torch.nn.functional as F\nfrom torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline\n\ncuda_source = r'''\n#include <cuda_runtime.h>\n#include <torch/extension.h>\n#include <cmath>\n\nstruct WelfordAcc {\n float n;\n float mean;\n float m2;\n};\n\n__device__ inline WelfordAcc welford_combine(WelfordAcc a, WelfordAcc b) {\n WelfordAcc res;\n res.n = a.n + b.n;\n if (res.n == 0.0f) {\n res.mean = 0.0f;\n res.m2 = 0.0f;\n return res;\n }\n float delta = b.mean - a.mean;\n res.mean = a.mean + delta * b.n / res.n;\n res.m2 = a.m2 + b.m2 + delta * delta * a.n * b.n / res.n;\n return res;\n}\n\n__device__ inline WelfordAcc warp_reduce_welford(WelfordAcc val) {\n for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) {\n WelfordAcc other;\n other.n = __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val.n, offset);\n other.mean = __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val.mean, offset);\n other.m2 = __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val.m2, offset);\n val = welford_combine(val, other);\n }\n return val;\n}\n\n__global__ void fused_kernel(\n const float* __restrict__ input,\n const float* __restrict__ gamma,\n const float* __restrict__ beta,\n float* __restrict__ output,\n int B, int C, int D, int H, int W, int num_groups, float eps) {\n\n extern __shared__ float s[];\n int b = blockIdx.x;\n int g = blockIdx.y;\n int cpg = C / num_groups;\n int c0 = g * cpg;\n int spatial_size = D * H * W;\n int num_warps = (blockDim.x + 31) >> 5;\n\n float* s_n = s;\n float* s_mean = s + num_warps * cpg;\n float* s_m2 = s + 2 * num_warps * cpg;\n float* group_mean_ptr = s + 3 * num_warps * cpg;\n float* group_var_ptr = group_mean_ptr + 1;\n float* final_mean = group_var_ptr + 1;\n\n int lane = threadIdx.x & 31;\n int wid = threadIdx.x >> 5;\n\n WelfordAcc acc[16];\n #pragma unroll\n for (int rc = 0; rc < cpg; rc++) {\n acc[rc].n = 0.0f;\n acc[rc].mean = 0.0f;\n acc[rc].m2 = 0.0f;\n }\n\n for (int s_idx = threadIdx.x; s_idx < spatial_size; s_idx += blockDim.x) {\n #pragma unroll\n for (int rc = 0; rc < cpg; rc++) {\n int c = c0 + rc;\n float x = input[(b * C + c) * spatial_size + s_idx];\n x = x * fminf(fmaxf(x + 3.0f, 0.0f), 6.0f) / 6.0f;\n\n acc[rc].n += 1.0f;\n float delta = x - acc[rc].mean;\n acc[rc].mean += delta / acc[rc].n;\n float delta2 = x - acc[rc].mean;\n acc[rc].m2 += delta * delta2;\n }\n }\n\n #pragma unroll\n for (int rc = 0; rc < cpg; rc++) {\n WelfordAcc lane_res = warp_reduce_welford(acc[rc]);\n if (lane == 0) {\n s_n[wid * cpg + rc] = lane_res.n;\n s_mean[wid * cpg + rc] = lane_res.mean;\n s_m2[wid * cpg + rc] = lane_res.m2;\n }\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n if (threadIdx.x == 0) {\n WelfordAcc group_acc;\n group_acc.n = 0.0f;\n group_acc.mean = 0.0f;\n group_acc.m2 = 0.0f;\n\n #pragma unroll\n for (int rc = 0; rc < cpg; rc++) {\n WelfordAcc ch_acc;\n ch_acc.n = 0.0f;\n ch_acc.mean = 0.0f;\n ch_acc.m2 = 0.0f;\n for (int w = 0; w < num_warps; w++) {\n WelfordAcc tmp;\n tmp.n = s_n[w * cpg + rc];\n tmp.mean = s_mean[w * cpg + rc];\n tmp.m2 = s_m2[w * cpg + rc];\n ch_acc = welford_combine(ch_acc, tmp);\n }\n final_mean[rc] = ch_acc.mean;\n group_acc = welford_combine(group_acc, ch_acc);\n }\n *group_mean_ptr = group_acc.mean;\n *group_var_ptr = (group_acc.n > 0.0f) ? (group_acc.m2 / group_acc.n) : 0.0f;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n float mean = *group_mean_ptr;\n float var = fmaxf(*group_var_ptr, 0.0f);\n float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps);\n\n if (threadIdx.x < cpg) {\n int rc = threadIdx.x;\n int c = c0 + rc;\n float cmean = final_mean[rc];\n output[b * C + c] = ((cmean - mean) * inv_std) * gamma[c] + beta[c];\n }\n}\n\nvoid fused_forward(\n torch::Tensor input,\n torch::Tensor gamma,\n torch::Tensor beta,\n torch::Tensor output,\n int num_groups,\n float eps) {\n\n int B = input.size(0);\n int C = input.size(1);\n int D = input.size(2);\n int H = input.size(3);\n int W = input.size(4);\n int cpg = C / num_groups;\n int num_warps = (256 + 31) >> 5;\n\n dim3 grid(B, num_groups);\n dim3 block(256);\n int shared_mem = (3 * num_warps * cpg + 2 + cpg) * sizeof(float);\n\n fused_kernel<<<grid, block, shared_mem>>>(\n input.data_ptr<float>(),\n gamma.data_ptr<float>(),\n beta.data_ptr<float>(),\n output.data_ptr<float>(),\n B, C, D, H, W, num_groups, eps\n );\n}\n'''\n\ncpp_source = r'''\nvoid fused_forward(\n torch::Tensor input,\n torch::Tensor gamma,\n torch::Tensor beta,\n torch::Tensor output,\n int num_groups,\n float eps);\n'''\n\nfused_ops = load_inline(\n name=\"fused_ops_v2\",\n cpp_sources=cpp_source,\n cuda_sources=cuda_source,\n functions=[\"fused_forward\"],\n extra_cuda_cflags=[\"-O3\", \"--use_fast_math\"],\n verbose=False,\n)\n\nclass ModelNew(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, num_groups=4, bias=True):\n super(ModelNew, self).__init__()\n self.conv = nn.Conv3d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, bias=bias)\n self.group_norm = nn.GroupNorm(num_groups, out_channels)\n\n def forward(self, x):\n x = self.conv(x)\n B, C, D, H, W = x.shape\n out = torch.empty(B, C, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype)\n fused_ops.fused_forward(\n x,\n self.group_norm.weight,\n self.group_norm.bias,\n out,\n self.group_norm.num_groups,\n self.group_norm.eps,\n )\n return out\n" }

Normalize channels within each group My kernel currently: - Computes per-channel means (after hardswish) - Then combines to get group mean and variance - Normalizes the MEAN (not individual elements) by GN, then writes the final channel mean Wait, that's NOT what the reference does! Let me re-read the reference: ```python x = self.conv(x) # (B, C, D, H, W) x = F.hardswish(x) # Nonlinear activation x = self.group_norm(x) # Normalization over channels x = torch.mean(x, dim=[2, 3, 4]) # Mean over spatial dims → (B, C) ``` So it does: 1. Conv3d 2. Hardswish (elementwise) 3. GroupNorm (applies normalization: (x - mean) / std, then affine transform γ, β) 4. MEAN over spatial dims So the output is MEAN of the normalized+scaled values, NOT normalized+scaled of the mean! So I need to: 1. For each channel, compute the mean and m2 of HardSwish output 2. Compute group stats from all channels in the group 3. Then for each element, apply GN normalization: (x - group_mean) / sqrt(group_var + eps) * gamma + beta 4. Then average this across spatial dimensions Or equivalently: the final output for channel c is: mean_over_space( GN(hardswish(conv(x))) ) Since GN is affine within each group, and mean is linear: mean(GN(x)) = GN(mean(x)) only if gamma and beta are constant. But they vary per channel! So: mean_over_space( (x - μ_g)/σ_g * γ_c + β_c ) = mean(x)/σ_g * γ_c - μ_g/σ_g * γ_c + β_c = (μ_c - μ_g)/σ_g * γ_c + β_c Where μ_c is the mean of channel c, and μ_g is the mean of all channels in the group! Wait, that IS correct: mean_over_space( (x_ij - μ_g)/σ_g * γ_c + β_c ) = (mean_over_space(x_ij) - μ_g)/σ_g * γ_c + β_c = (μ_c - μ_g)/σ_g * γ_c + β_c Where μ_c = mean_over_space(x_ij) and μ_g is the GN group mean over the whole group! And σ_g² = variance of all elements in the group = mean(x²) - μ_g² Wait, but variance for GN is computed across all elements in all channels in the group: E[x²] - E[x]², where E is over all spatial dims and all channels in the group. So I need to compute: - μ_c for each channel c = mean of hardswish(conv(x)) over spatial dims - E_g[x] = mean over all channels in group, all spatial dims - E_g[x²] = mean over all channels in group, all spatial dims of squared values - σ_g = sqrt(E_g[x²] - E_g[x]² + eps) Then output_c = ((μ_c - E_g[x]) / σ_g) * γ_c + β_c So my kernel logic was actually CORRECT conceptually! But my Welford accumulator computes variance, not mean of squared values. Let me check if Welford M2 / n gives the population variance... Welford's algorithm with M2 = sum of squared differences from mean: - var = M2 / n gives population variance (i.e., E[(x - μ)²]) Yes! That's correct for GN. So the logic is: 1. Compute per-channel mean and M2 with Welford 2. Combine all channels in group to get group mean and group M2 3. group_var = group_M2 / total_n 4. sigma = sqrt(group_var + eps) Then output_c = ((channel_mean_c - group_mean) / sigma) * gamma_c + beta_c My kernel does exactly this! Let me verify my use of Welford: For each element x: - n += 1 - delta = x - mean - mean += delta / n - delta2 = x - mean - m2 += delta * delta2 This is correct Welford. For population variance, var = m2 / n. In my code: ```c *group_var_ptr = (group_acc.n > 0.0f) ? (group_acc.m2 / group_acc.n) : 0.0f; ``` Yes, this is correct for population variance. And: ```c float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); ``` Then: ```c float out_val = ((cmean - mean) * inv_std) * gamma[c] + beta[c]; ``` Yes, this is correct! The means are per-channel means, and we normalize with group stats. But wait, I computed `cmean` (channel mean after hardswish) via Welford. And group_acc is combined from all channel Welford results. When combining Welford accumulators across channels: - a.n = total elements for channel a over spatial - a.mean = mean over spatial - b.n = total elements for channel b over spatial - b.mean = mean over spatial If I combine them, I get mean over spatial and channels, which is correct for group mean! And m2? When combining Welford accumulators: m2 is computed such that m2 / n gives variance. When combining multiple groups of points, the combined m2 also satisfies combined_m2 / combined_n = variance over all points. Yes, Welford combination preserves this property. So group_acc.m2 / group_acc.n = variance over all elements in all channels in the group. My logic is correct. Let me run the corrected code through correctness check. The kernel passes correctness but has high max relative error (8.41). That's concerning. It might be a case where the reference output is very close to 0. Let me think about the output of this model. The model does Conv3D -> HardSwish -> GroupNorm -> Mean. For the mean, after GroupNorm it could indeed be close to zero for some channels because the weights in layer might not be normalized. But the max abs error is 4.77e-07 which is well within fp32 tolerance (1e-4). So correctness passes. I should think about maximizing performance. The kernel currently has each thread processing multiple spatial points for each channel, which is good. But there are a few issues: 1. The unroll loop `for (int rc = 0; rc < cpg; rc++)` is unrolled but cpg=4 channels per group. The thread processes all 4 channels in the same loop, but the #pragma unroll might help. 2. One issue: each warp processes contiguous threads, and each thread reads spatial_size elements across a stride. For each spatial element, we load 4 separate channels. This is good for coalescing because thread 0, 1, 2... read adjacent spatial positions for the same channel - but actually, threadIdx.x accesses different spatial indices, so adjacent threads read adjacent memory positions for the same channel. That's coalesced. 3. However, when we do `for (int rc=0; rc<cpg; rc++)`, we're reading different channels for the same spatial_idx. This is strided by spatial_size but within the same batch element. This is also coalesced if the warp is doing memory transactions together? Let's see: if thread 0 reads spatial_idx 0 for channel c0, thread 1 reads spatial_idx 1 for channel c0 (adjacent), etc. So the memory requests are adjacent within each channel loop iteration. For the next rc, thread 0 reads spatial_idx 0 for channel c0+1, which is offset by spatial_size but still coalesced if the warp size accesses contiguous. Actually, each 32-thread warp accesses 32 consecutive floats for a given rc. The next rc starts at a different base address but thread 0 will request base2+0, thread1 base2+1, etc. This is also coalesced, but causes 4 separate loads per iteration of the outer spatial loop. 4. There are uncoalesced stores into shared memory for `s_n[wid*cpg + rc]`, `s_mean[wid*cpg + rc]`, `s_m2[wid*cpg + rc]`. Each warp (wid) writes a contiguous chunk of cpg floats. Within the warp, lane==0 writes, others don't, so no bank conflicts. But we're using 3 separate shared memory arrays. Could be slightly better to combine them but it's fine. 5. There are multiple blocks (B=1024, num_groups=4 => 4096 blocks). Each SM on H100 can run 32 blocks, but we have 132 SMs? Actually H100 has 132 or 144 SMs? The spec says 80GB HBM3 has 132 SMs. 4096 blocks / 132 = about 31 blocks per SM initially, which is a good amount. 6. Shared memory usage: (3 * num_warps * cpg + 2 + cpg) * sizeof(float) = (3*8*4 + 2 + 4)*4 = (96+6)*4 = 408 bytes per block, very low. 7. The kernel is probably memory bound because we're just reading input once, writing output once. The main cost is just reading the input tensor: B*C*D*H*W floats = 1024*16*16*32*32*4 bytes = 1,048,576 KB ≈ 1GB. At 3.35TB/s, this would take about 0.3ms. But we also have the conv3d cost before this. However, looking at the task description more carefully, it's asking to replace the forward computation after conv. Actually wait, the model is: ```python x = self.conv(x) x = F.hardswish(x) x = self.group_norm(x) x = torch.mean(x, dim=[2,3,4]) ``` The problem is the Conv3D itself takes a lot of time. Since we replace the forward computation, we still have conv3d preceding it. I should check if we need to fuse the conv3d as well. The PyTorch reference `self.conv(x)` is a standard cudnn op that's pretty optimal. But the subsequent ops (hardswish, GN, mean) could be fused together to save bandwidth. Wait, looking at the original problem statement: "Replace the forward computation of the PyTorch model below with a custom CUDA kernel." The model forward includes conv3d, hardswish, group_norm, mean. Should we replace just part of it, or the whole thing? The instructions say to replace the forward computation with a custom CUDA kernel. But conv3d is quite complex to implement manually. However, if the evaluation is timing `ModelNew.forward()`, and `ModelNew` might use PyTorch conv3d plus our fused kernel, vs the reference using PyTorch conv3d + 3 op calls, the speedup comes from fusing these 3 ops. Since conv3d is common to both, the ratio might still be decent. But maybe we should try to fuse conv3d into our kernel too for maximum speedup. However, implementing a 3D convolution that competes with cuDNN is tricky, especially with a kernel_size=4, stride=1, etc. But note: the output size after conv3d is (B, C_out, D_out, H_out, W_out). Given input is (1024, 3, 16, 32, 32), kernel_size=4, with stride=1 and no padding specified (default=0), the output dimensions are: D_out = 16 - 4 + 1 = 13 H_out = 32 - 4 + 1 = 29 W_out = 32 - 4 + 1 = 29 Wait, this is different from what I assumed earlier. And output channels = 16. The original reference reference dimensions: input: (1024, 3, 16, 32, 32) conv3d(in_channels=3, out_channels=16, kernel_size=4) output size: (1024, 16, 13, 29, 29) So the spatial size H*W*D = 13*29*29 = 10933. The mean is taken over dims [2,3,4], producing (1024, 16). Our fused kernel currently assumes the input is already after conv. That's fine. But if I want to fuse conv3d too, I'd need to know the conv weights and bias. They are model parameters. I could read them in the kernel and implement a simple 3D convolution inline. But this would be much more complex and might not beat cuDNN. Given the complexity, let's first stick to the fused hardswish+GN+mean approach. The wins should be good. Let me consider if there's a better approach. Instead of computing per-block reductions for each channel, I could just compute the mean and m2 of all output channels in the group directly. Note: In GroupNorm, we need the mean and variance of all elements in the group. But we also need the per-channel means for the final reduction because the output is the mean of each channel, not the normalized values. However, I realize that we are computing the mean of each channel (after hardswish) to output. But we need group statistics for normalizing the entire group's mean? Wait, the reference is: ```python x = F.hardswish(x) # (B, C, D, H, W) x = self.group_norm(x) # (B, C, D, H, W) x = torch.mean(x, dim=[2,3,4]) # (B, C) ``` So the output is the per-channel mean of the *group-normalized* tensor. This means: 1. Apply HardSwish 2. Compute GroupNorm over each group 3. For each channel, the output is the mean of all spatial points of the normalized tensor. Since the normalization is affine: x_norm = (x - mu_g) / sigma_g * gamma + beta The spatial mean of x_norm is: mean(x_norm) = mean((x - mu_g)/sigma_g * gamma + beta) = (mean(x) - mu_g)/sigma_g * gamma + beta where mean(x) is the spatial mean of the hardswish-activated channel, mu_g is the group mean, and sigma_g is the group std. So indeed, we only need the per-channel means of the hardswish output, the group mean and variance, and then apply the formula. We don't need to normalize every element and then compute the mean. This saves a lot of computation. Our kernel does exactly this. However, I'm computing Welford on the hardswish output to get per-channel mean and group mean/variance. But I can compute the per-channel mean with a simple sum reduction, and group variance by accumulating squared differences. Welford is used for numerical stability but with 10933 elements, a simple sum should be fine in float32. Moreover, since we only need variance of the group, we could compute sum(x) and sum(x^2) in a fused manner. Let me simplify: for each channel, compute sum(x) (which gives mean), and also during the same pass, contribute to group_sum and group_sum2 for variance. Actually variance of group = E[x^2] - E[x]^2. So we can compute: - group_sum: sum of all elements in group - group_sum2: sum of squares of all elements in group - channel_sum: sum of all elements per channel Then: - group_mean = group_sum / (cpg * spatial) - group_var = group_sum2 / (cpg * spatial) - group_mean^2 And output for channel c: - out_c = ((channel_sum_c / spatial) - group_mean) / sqrt(group_var + eps) * gamma_c + beta_c This is much simpler than Welford and might be faster because we avoid the complex Welford state updates and shared memory layout. Also Welford does 3 shuffles per element across the warp. With sum and sum2, we can reduce warp sums with simple __shfl_down on floats, which is faster. Let me rewrite the kernel with this simpler approach. But note: using sum and sum2 could have numerical issues for variance, but with float32 and 10933 values, it should be fine. The tolerance is 1e-4. Also, I can pre-compute `inv_spatial = 1.0f / spatial_size`. Let me structure the kernel differently for maximum efficiency: 1. Each block handles one batch element and one group. 2. threads in the block iterate over spatial indices. 3. For each channel in the group (cpg=4), each thread maintains: - sum for that channel - sum2 for that channel (needed for group variance) 4. Actually, for group variance I need sum of squares over all channels in the group. I can compute sum of sum2 across channels. Implementation: ```cpp float ch_sum[16]; // up to 16 channels per group float ch_sum2[16]; // sum of squares per channel (needed for group variance) // initialize for rc in 0..cpg-1: ch_sum[rc] = 0.0f; ch_sum2[rc] = 0.0f; // iterate over spatial for s_idx = threadIdx.x; s_idx < spatial_size; s_idx += blockDim.x: #pragma unroll for rc in 0..cpg-1: float x = input[base + s_idx]; x = hardswish(x); ch_sum[rc] += x; ch_sum2[rc] += x * x; // warp reduce for rc in 0..cpg-1: ch_sum[rc] = warp_reduce_sum(ch_sum[rc]); ch_sum2[rc] = warp_reduce_sum(ch_sum2[rc]); // write to shared mem if lane == 0: s_sum[wid*cpg + rc] = ch_sum[rc]; s_sum2[wid*cpg + rc] = ch_sum2[rc]; __syncthreads(); // one thread block reduce from shared mem if threadIdx.x == 0: group_sum = 0, group_sum2 = 0; for rc in 0..cpg-1: float total_sum = 0; float total_sum2 = 0; for w in 0..num_warps-1: total_sum += s_sum[w*cpg+rc]; total_sum2 += s_sum2[w*cpg+rc]; s_final_sum[rc] = total_sum; group_sum += total_sum; group_sum2 += total_sum2; float inv_spatial = 1.0f / (cpg * spatial_size); float group_mean = group_sum * inv_spatial; float group_var = group_sum2 * inv_spatial - group_mean * group_mean; s_group_mean = group_mean; s_group_var = group_var; __syncthreads(); // final output group_mean = s_group_mean; group_var = max(s_group_var, 0.0f); inv_std = rsqrtf(group_var + eps); inv_spatial_ch = 1.0f / spatial_size; if threadIdx.x < cpg: rc = threadIdx.x; c = c0 + rc; chmean = s_final_sum[rc] * inv_spatial_ch; out = ((chmean - group_mean) * inv_std) * gamma[c] + beta[c]; ``` This should be faster because warp_reduce_sum is much simpler than warp_reduce_welford. Also, we can make the block size 256 threads, which means each thread handles about 10933/256 ≈ 43 elements. That's reasonable. One thing to check: can we use __shfl_down_sync for float sum? Yes. Let me also consider if we should merge this into a single kernel or keep it as a separate call. Given the constraints, separate is fine. Also I might want to fuse conv3d too. Let's consider that. The conv3d kernel_size=4, in_channels=3, out_channels=16. The output after conv is (1024, 16, 13, 29, 29). Weights shape: (16, 3, 4, 4, 4). Bias: (16,). To fuse conv3d + hardswish + GN + mean, for each output channel and batch element, I need to convolve over the input 3x13x29x29 region with a 3x4x4x4 filter. But note the output spatial size is only 13x29x29 = 10933 positions per channel. With 16 channels and 1024 batches, total output positions = 1024*16*10933 = 179M. Each output position requires 3*4*4*4 = 192 multiply-adds. Total FLOPs = 179M * 192 ≈ 34.4 GFLOPs. In FP32, the H100 does 67 TFLOPs, so pure compute time would be ~0.5ms for the conv. But the bandwidth for reading input is: the input size is 1024*3*16*32*32*4 = 201MB. Reading once takes ~0.06ms. So the conv is compute bound. cuDNN likely achieves high utilization for this. If I implement conv3d in the same kernel, I'd need to load the input once and compute the convolution on the fly while accumulating statistics. However, implementing an efficient conv3d from scratch that matches cuDNN is hard, especially with the filter size=4. We'd need to handle tiling, shared memory for inputs, etc. Given we have limited turns and implementation complexity, I will NOT fuse conv3d. I'll rely on cuDNN for the conv and fuse the rest. The speedup will come from avoiding multiple kernel launches and intermediate memory traffic. Wait, but if the evaluation includes the conv3d time in ModelNew.forward(), and the reference also includes it, then the total time difference will be mostly from the fused part. If conv3d takes, say, 1ms, and the rest takes 2ms in reference but 0.5ms in our version, the overall speedup would be 3ms vs 1.5ms = 2x. That's decent. However, I'm noticing the correctness error is coming from somewhere. The max_abs_err is small but max_rel_err is high. This is likely because some output values are near zero (e.g., when gamma is small or mean cancels out). In float32, the relative error can blow up for near-zero values, but the absolute error is still tiny. So it should pass. Let me also consider whether to compute shared memory differently or use shared memory in a more bank-conflict-free way. Shared memory layout: ``` float* s_sum = s; float* s_sum2 = s + num_warps * cpg; float* s_final_sum = s + 2 * num_warps * cpg; float* s_group_mean = s_final_sum + cpg; float* s_group_var = s_group_mean + 1; ``` For cpg=4, num_warps=8: - s_sum: 32 floats - s_sum2: 32 floats - s_final_sum: 4 floats - s_group_mean: 1 float - s_group_var: 1 float Total: 70 floats = 280 bytes. When writing `s_sum[wid*cpg + rc]`, each warp writes adjacent floats (wid*cpg to wid*cpg+cpg-1). Since cpg=4, each warp writes 4 floats, which fit in 1 bank per float but there are 32 banks, so no bank conflict. Let me also consider using `__ldg` for reading input. On H100, this helps with read-only data caching from global memory. However, I need to ensure the pointer is const __restrict__. I'll use that. One more thing: I should ensure that the grid dimensions and occupancy are good. With 4096 blocks and 132 SMs, each SM gets about 31 blocks. With block size 256, register limit is 65536, so max threads per SM is limited. If each thread uses few registers, we can get good occupancy. Let me rewrite the kernel with the simpler sum/sum2 approach. Actually, I realize one more thing: I need to be careful about shared memory size with dynamic allocation. Currently I compute it at runtime. But some of the arrays need alignment. Using the simple contiguous layout is fine as long as there's enough. Let me write the new kernel.

<cuda_runtime.h>\n#include <torch/extension.h>\n#include <cmath>\n\n__device__ inline float warp_reduce_sum(float val) {\n #pragma unroll\n for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) {\n val += group_sum / group_nelem;\n float group_var = group_sum2 / group_nelem - group_mean * group_mean;\n *s_group_mean = group_mean;\n *s_group_var = fmaxf(group_var, 0.0f);\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n float
